(1) الحَمْدُ للهِ الَّذِي قَدْ أَخْرَجَا نَتَائِجَ الفِكْرِ لِأَرْبَابِ الحِجَا. وَعَلَى جَعْلِهَا لِلِاسْتِغْرَاقِ، أَوْ لِلجِنْسِ فِي ضِمْنِ الأَفْرَادِ؛ يَمْتَنِعُ ذَلِكَ (1) بِالنِّسْبَةِ لِلقَدِيمِ، وَلَا يَمْتَنِعُ بِالنِّسْبَةِ لِلحَادِثِ إِنْ لُوحِظَ أَنَّ الأَفْرَادَ غَيْرُ مُرَكَّبَةٍ، وَإِلَّا لَمْ يَمْتَنِعُ أَصْلاً (2)؛ لِمَا عَلِمْتَ مِنْ أَنَّ المُرَكَّبَ مِنَ القَدِيم وَالحَادِثِ حَادِفٌ. وَمِمَّا يَنْبَغِي التَّنْبَهَ لَهُ : أَنَّ الحَمْدَ القَدِيمَ هُوَ نَفْسُ الكَلَامِ القَدِيمِ بِاعْتِبَارِ دَلَالَتِهِ عَلَى الكَمَالَاتِ، فَهُوَ مِنْ أَنْوَاعِ الكَلَامِ الاعْتِبَارِيَّةِ (3) ، كَمَا هُوَ مُقَرَّرٌ فِي عِلْمِ التَّوْحِيدِ. وَقَدِ اشْتُهِرَ أَيْضاً أَنَّ جُمْلَةَ الحَمْدَلَةِ» يَصِحُ أَنْ تَكُونَ إِنْشَائِيَّةٌ ، وَعَلَيْهِ فَلَا تُسَمَّى : «قَضِيَّةٌ»؛ لِمَا مَرَّ ، وَأَنْ تَكُونَ خَبَرِيَّةٌ ، وَعَلَيْهِ فَتُسَمَّى: «قَضِيَّةٌ» ; ثُمَّ: - إِنْ جُعِلَتْ «أَلْ» فِيهَا لِلعَهْدِ، كَانَتْ «قَضِيَّةٌ شَخْصِيَّةٌ» . - وَإِنْ جُعِلَتْ لِلِاسْتِغْرَاقِ، كَانَتْ «قَضِيَّةٌ كُلِّيَّةٌ (4)) . - وَإِنْ جُعِلَتْ لِلجِنْسِ فِي ضِمْنِ البَعْضِ ، كَانَتْ «قَضِيَّةٌ جُزْئِيَّةٌ ( ه ) . وَإِنْ جُعِلَتْ لَهُ فِي ضِمْنِ الأَفْرَادِ بِقَطْعِ النَّظَرِ عَنِ الكُلِّيَّةِ وَالجُزْئِيَّةِ، كَانَتْ «قَضِيَّةٌ مُهْمَلَةٌ» (1). - وَلَا مَانِعَ هُنَا مِنْ جَعْلِهَا طَبِيعِيَّة) (2)؛ بِأَنْ تُجْعَلَ أَلْ» فِيهَا لِلجِنْسِ وَالطَّبِيعَةِ، بِقَطْعِ النَّظَرِ عَنِ الْأَفْرَادِ. وَاسْتُشْكِلَ كَوْنَهَا إِنْشَائِيَّةٌ : بِأَنَّهُ لَا يُمْكِنُ العَبْدَ أَنْ يُنْشِئَ مَضْمُونَ هَذِهِ الجُمْلَةِ وَلَا حُكْمَهَا :

Definition of Natijah and Critique of Al-Malawi
Definition of natijah and critique of a common definition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the technical definition of natijah (qawl) from the approximate tasdiq-based definition and explain why the distinction matters.

Core Idea
Natijah is the proposition that follows, not the act of assenting to it.

In logic, precision of terminology is critical. The term 'natijah' (conclusion) might seem straightforward, but a loose definition can blur the line between the logical consequence itself and our mental acceptance of it. This lesson unpacks the correct technical definition and highlights a common error made by some commentators, including al-Malawi in his Small Commentary.

Guided Path
1
Extract the linguistic meaning
Find the phrase that gives the linguistic meaning of 'natijah' (fruit/benefit). Note that this is the everyday sense, not the technical one.
2
Find the technical definition
Locate the technical definition: 'al-qawl al-lazim min taslim qawlayn li-dhatihima' (the statement necessarily following from the submission of two propositions for their own sake). 'Qawl' here means a proposition, not the mental act.
This definition is attributed to al-Malawi in his Great Commentary.
3
Identify al-Malawi's alternative definition
In his Small Commentary, al-Malawi defined natijah as 'al-tasdiq al-lazim...' (the assent necessarily following...). Identify this shift from 'qawl' to 'tasdiq'.
Tasdiq means assent or affirmation, a mental act.
4
Understand why it is approximate
The commentator al-Bajuri (the author of this passage) says this definition 'la yakhlu 'an tasammuh' (is not free from approximation). Why? Because the conclusion is the proposition itself (qawl), not the act of affirming it (tasdiq). Confusing them leads to a category error.
The author warns that some were deceived (ightarra) by this loose definition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قَوْلُهُ : (نَتَايْجَ الفِكْرِ) أَي: النَّتَائِجَ الَّتِي تَنْشَأَ عَنِ الفِكْرِ . وَالنَّتَائِجُ جَمْعُ: «نَتِيجَة» ، وَهِيَ لُغَةٌ : «الثَّمَرَةُ وَالفَائِدَةُ». وَاصْطِلاحاً : القَوْلُ اللَّازِمُ مِنْ تَسْلِيم قَوْلَيْنِ لِذَاتِهِمَا ؛ كَمَا يُصَرِّحُ بِهِ كَلَامُ الشَّيْخ المَلوِيِّ؛ فِي شَرْحِهِ الكَبِيرِ فِي بَابِ القِيَاسِ، فَتَفْسِيرُهُ لَهَا فِي شَرْحِهِ الصَّغِيرِ هنا : بِأَنَّهَا التَّصْدِيقُ اللَّازِمُ مِنْ تَسْلِيمٍ تَصْدِيقَيْنِ لِذَاتِهِمَا، لَا يَخْلُو عَنْ تَسَمُّحٍ، كَمَا نَصَّ عَلَيْهِ بَعْضُ المُحَقِّقِينَ، وَإِنِ اغْتَرَ بِهِ بَعْضُهُمْ، وَيُؤَيِّدُ ذَلِكَ قَوْلُ المُصَنِّفِ فِيمَا يَأْتِي :
His statement: 'the results of thought' i.e., the results that arise from thought. And 'al-nata'ij' is the plural of 'natijah', which linguistically means 'fruit and benefit'. And technically: the statement necessarily following from the submission of two propositions for their own sake, as is explicitly stated in the words of al-Malawi in his Great Commentary in the chapter on syllogism. So his interpretation of it in his Small Commentary here: that it is the assent necessarily following from the submission of two assents for their own sake, is not free from approximation, as some investigators have stated, and some have been deceived by it. And that is supported by the author's statement in what comes:
Source pages p. 61

Commentary
This passage defines the term 'natijah' (result/conclusion) as used in logic. It begins by glossing the author's phrase 'nata'ij al-fikr' (results of thought) as the results that arise from thought. Then it gives the linguistic meaning of 'natijah': fruit and benefit. The technical definition, according to al-Malawi in his Great Commentary on the Sullam, is: 'the statement (qawl) necessarily following from the submission (taslim) of two propositions for their own sake.' This is the standard definition of a syllogistic conclusion. The passage then critiques al-Malawi's definition in his Small Commentary, where he defined it as 'the assent (tasdiq) necessarily following from the submission of two assents for their own sake.' The author of this commentary (al-Bajuri) considers this definition approximate (tasammuh) because a conclusion is a statement (qawl meaning a proposition), not the act of assenting to it. Some later commentators were misled by this loose definition. The passage ends by noting that the author's own upcoming statement supports the correct definition. This shows the precision required in logical terminology: 'natijah' is the proposition that is the necessary consequence of premises, not the mental act of affirming it.

Key Terms
نتيجة
تصديق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is al-Malawi's definition of natijah as 'tasdiq' considered approximate?
A) Because tasdiq is a broader term that includes non-syllogistic assent
B) Because the correct definition requires the conclusion to be a qawl (proposition), not a mental act
C) Because al-Malawi derived it from a different logical tradition
D) Because it contradicts the linguistic meaning of 'fruit and benefit'

Key Takeaway
The conclusion (natijah) of a syllogism is the proposition that necessarily follows from the premises, not the mental act of assenting to it.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

The Technical Definition of Natijah
Author defines natijah technically and corrects a prior definition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the precise technical definition of natijah and explain why defining it as 'tasdiq' is considered imprecise.

Core Idea
A conclusion is a statement, not merely an assent.

In this passage, the commentator clarifies the exact meaning of 'natijah' as it appears in the opening line of the Sullam. While some logicians defined it loosely as 'assent', the precise definition requires it to be a 'statement' (qawl) that necessarily follows from the admission of two premises for their own sake. Understanding this distinction is key to grasping the mechanics of qiyas.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term
Locate the word 'natijah' in the Arabic text. Note that the plural 'nata'ij' appears in the matn line 'nata'ij al-fikr'.
The term is defined both linguistically and technically.
2
Extract the technical definition
Find the phrase 'al-qawl al-lazim min taslim qawlayn li dhatihima'. This is the precise technical definition.
The definition includes four elements: qawl (statement), lazim (necessary), min taslim qawlayn (from admission of two statements), li dhatihima (for their own sake).
3
Compare with the imprecise definition
The text mentions that al-Malawi's Small Commentary defined natijah as 'tasdiq' (assent) instead of 'qawl'. Understand why this is imprecise.
Tasdiq is the mental act of assenting, while qawl is the linguistic proposition. The conclusion is the proposition that is assented to, not the act itself.
4
Understand the correction
The author supports the correct definition by citing the matn's later verse that qiyas yields 'qawl'an akhar' (another statement). This confirms that the result is a statement.
This correction is important for distinguishing between the cognitive process and its logical product.

الحَمْدُ للهِ الَّذِي قَدْ أَخْرَجَا نَتَائِجَ الفِكْرِ لِأَرْبَابِ الحِجَـا (متن). قَوْلُهُ : (نَتَائِجَ الفِكْرِ) أَي: النَّتَائِجَ الَّتِي تَنْشَأَ عَنِ الفِكْرِ. وَالنَّتَائِجُ جَمْعُ: «نَتِيجَة» وَهِيَ لُغَةٌ : «الثَّمَرَةُ وَالفَائِدَةُ». وَاصْطِلاحاً : القَوْلُ اللَّازِمُ مِنْ تَسْلِيم قَوْلَيْنِ لِذَاتِهِمَا؛ كَمَا يُصَرِّحُ بِهِ كَلَامُ الشَّيْخ المَلوِيِّ فِي شَرْحِهِ الكَبِيرِ فِي بَابِ القِيَاسِ. فَتَفْسِيرُهُ لَهَا فِي شَرْحِهِ الصَّغِيرِ هنا : بِأَنَّهَا التَّصْدِيقُ اللَّازِمُ مِنْ تَسْلِيمٍ تَصْدِيقَيْنِ لِذَاتِهِمَا، لَا يَخْلُو عَنْ تَسَمُّحٍ. وَيُؤَيِّدُ ذَلِكَ قَوْلُ المُصَنِّفِ فِيمَا يَأْتِي : إِنَّ القِيَاسَ مِنْ قَضَايَا صُوِّرًا مُسْتَلْزِماً بِالذَّاتِ قَوْلاً آخَرَا. وَإِنَّمَا قَالُوا : مِنْ تَسْلِيم ... إلخ؛ إِشَارَةٌ إِلَى أَنَّهُ لَا يُشْتَرَطُ حِقِّيَّتُهُمَا، بَلِ المَدَارُ عَلَى تَسْلِيمِهِمَا، وَلَوْ كَانَا جَهْلاً، كَمَا لَوْ قَالَ قَائِلٌ : العَالَمُ قَدِيمٌ، وَكُلُّ مَنْ كَانَ كَذَلِكَ فَلَا بُدَّ لَهُ مِنْ موجد»، فَإِنَّهُ يَلْزَمُ مِنْ تَسْلِيمٍ هَذَيْنِ القَوْلَيْنِ - مَعَ كَوْنِهِمَا جَهْلاً فِي الوَاقِعِ - أَنْ يُقَالَ: «العَالَمُ لَا بُدَّ لَهُ مِنْ مُوجِده». وَخَرَجَ بِقَيْدِ «لِذَاتِهِمَا » : القَوْلُ اللَّازِمُ مِنْ تَسْلِيمِ قَوْلَينِ لَا لِذَاتِهِمَا، بَلْ لِأَمْرٍ خَارِج؛ كَمَا فِي قَوْلِهِمْ : زَيْدٌ مُسَاوِ لِعَمْرٍو، وَعَمْرُو مُسَاءٍ لِبَكْرِه، فَإِنَّهُ يَلْزَمُ مِنْ تَسْلِيمٍ هَذَيْنِ القَوْلَيْنِ أَنْ يُقَالَ: ...
Praise be to Allah who brought forth the results of thought for the people of insight. His statement: 'results of thought' means the results that arise from thought. 'Results' is the plural of 'result', which linguistically means 'fruit' and 'benefit'. Technically: it is the statement necessarily following from the admission of two statements for their own sake, as is clarified by the speech of al-Malawi in his Great Commentary in the chapter on qiyas. His interpretation of it in his Small Commentary here: that it is the assent necessarily following from the admission of two assents for their own sake, is not free from imprecision. This is supported by the author's statement later: 'Indeed, qiyas consists of premises arranged so as to necessarily entail another statement by essence.' They said 'from admission...' etc., indicating that their truth is not required, but rather the basis is on their admission, even if they are ignorance, as when someone says: 'The world is eternal, and everything that is so must have a creator', then it follows from the admission of these two statements—despite them being ignorance in reality—that it is said: 'The world must have a creator.' And excluded by the condition 'for their own sake' is the statement necessarily following from the admission of two statements not for their own sake, but due to an external matter, as in their saying: 'Zayd is equal to Amr, and Amr is equal to Bakr', for it follows from the admission of these two statements that it is said: ...
Source pages p. 61

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the opening line of the Sullam al-Munawnaq, defining the term 'natijah' (result/conclusion). The commentator first gives the linguistic meaning: fruit or benefit. Then the technical definition: a statement (qawl) that necessarily follows from the admission of two statements for their own sake (li dhatihima). This definition is from al-Malawi's Great Commentary. However, al-Malawi in his Small Commentary defined it as 'assent' (tasdiq) rather than 'statement', which the commentator notes is imprecise because it suggests the conclusion is merely grasping the relation (which is the meaning of tasdiq). The correct view is that the conclusion is a statement that is assented to. This is supported by the author's later verse that qiyas is composed of premises arranged to necessarily entail another statement by essence. The phrase 'for their own sake' means the necessity arises from the intrinsic content of the two statements, not from an external factor. Thus, truth of the premises is not required; only their admission in the reasoning process matters. The example given is: 'The world is eternal, and everything eternal must have a creator.' Even though both premises are false (the world is not eternal, and if it were, it would not need a creator), if one admits them, the conclusion 'the world must have a creator' follows. The condition 'for their own sake' excludes cases where the conclusion follows due to an external relation. The example: 'Zayd is equal to Amr, and Amr is equal to Bakr.' This does not yield a necessary conclusion because the middle term is not repeated—the predicate of the first is not the subject of the second in a universal way. The necessity here is not intrinsic to the statements but relies on an external property of equality. The commentary then points out that this example shows the need for 'takarur al-hadd al-awsat' (repetition of the middle term) for a valid syllogism.

نتيجة
لذاتهما
تسليم

A) The fruit or benefit of reasoning.
B) The assent necessarily following from the admission of two assents for their own sake.
C) The statement necessarily following from the admission of two statements for their own sake.
D) The conclusion that is true in reality.

Key Takeaway
The technical definition of natijah is: a statement that necessarily follows from the admission of two statements for their own sake.

The Condition of Intrinsic Necessity (Li Dhatihima)
Author explains the condition 'li dhatihima' with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the condition 'li dhatihima' and identify cases where the necessity is extrinsic, such as the Zayd-Amr-Bakr example.

Core Idea
Necessity must arise from the premises themselves, not from an external relation.

The definition of natijah includes the critical phrase 'li dhatihima' (for their own sake). This condition distinguishes genuine syllogistic entailment from other types of inference where the conclusion follows due to an external factor. The commentator illustrates this with two examples: one where false premises still yield a necessary conclusion, and another where the conclusion follows from an external property (equality) rather than the intrinsic content of the premises.

Guided Path
1
Understand the meaning of 'li dhatihima'
The phrase means that the necessity of the conclusion must be due to the intrinsic meaning of the premises themselves, not due to an external factor. It is part of the definition of natijah.
2
Analyse the first example (al-ʿālam qadīm)
The premises 'The world is eternal' and 'Everything eternal must have a creator' are false in reality. Nevertheless, if one admits them, the conclusion 'The world must have a creator' follows necessarily. This shows that truth is not required; only admission matters. The necessity is intrinsic because it arises from the logical relation between the terms.
This example highlights that logical validity is independent of factual truth.
3
Analyse the second example (Zayd = Amr = Bakr)
The premises 'Zayd is equal to Amr' and 'Amr is equal to Bakr' yield the conclusion 'Zayd is equal to Bakr'. However, this necessity is not due to the intrinsic content of the premises alone; it relies on the transitivity of equality, which is an external property. Therefore, it does not satisfy 'li dhatihima'.
The commentator points out that this example illustrates the need for repetition of the middle term (takarur al-hadd al-awsat) for a valid syllogism.
4
Connect to the middle term requirement
In the second example, the middle term (the one that repeats) is not properly aligned: the predicate of the first (equal to Amr) and the subject of the second (Amr is equal) do not form the same middle term. In a valid syllogism, the middle term must be identical and repeated.
This condition ensures the necessity is intrinsic.

Defining Fikr: Linguistic and Terminological Senses
Author defines fikr as movement of the soul in intelligibles (linguistic) and arrangement of two things (terminological).

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the linguistic and terminological definitions of fikr (thought) and differentiate it from takhyil (imagination).

Core Idea
Thought is the soul's movement among intelligibles; reasoning is the arrangement of two premises to yield a conclusion.

Before a student of logic can master syllogisms and proofs, he must first understand what thought (fikr) itself is. The opening definition of this lesson sets the stage: fikr is the movement of the soul in intelligibles – not in sense‑perceptions, which belong to imagination (takhyil). Terminologically, it is the arrangement of two things, i.e., the basic act of inference. This distinction is the foundation of all logical reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Locate the two definitions
Find the linguistic (لغة) and terminological (اصطلاحاً) definitions in the Arabic text. Notice the clear contrast between the two.
2
Contrast fikr with takhyil
The author explicitly distinguishes fikr from takhyil (imagination) by domain: fikr deals with intelligibles (معقولات), takhyil with sensibles (محسوسات). Why is this separation important for logic?
3
Interpret the terminological definition
'Arrangement of two things' is a concise formula for the act of inference. Think of it as ordering two premises (or concepts) to derive a third piece of knowledge. How does this capture the core of syllogistic reasoning?
4
Connect to later topics
This definition of fikr underpins the entire study of qiyas (syllogism). When you later learn about categorical syllogisms, remember that each one is an instance of 'arrangement of two things' – two premises arranged to produce a conclusion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَالفِكْرُ لُغَةٌ : حَرَكَةُ النَّفْسِ فِي المَعْقُولَاتِ (۳) ، بِخِلَافِهَا فِي المَحْسُوسَاتِ، فَإِنَّهَا تَخَيُّل (٤) . وَاصْطِلاحاً : تَرْتِيبُ أَمْرَيْنِ
And fikr (thought) linguistically is: the movement of the soul in intelligibles, unlike its movement in sensibles, for that is imagination. And terminologically: the arrangement of two things.
Source pages p. 63

Commentary
This passage provides a two-part definition of fikr (thought). First, the linguistic (lugha) definition: 'movement of the soul in intelligibles (ma'qulat)'. This means that the soul moves or transitions from one intelligible concept to another, as when we move from a known premise to a conclusion. By contrast, movement in sensibles (mahsusat) is not called fikr but rather takhyil (imagination). This distinction emphasizes that thought is about concepts, not sense perceptions. The terminological (istilah) definition is more technical: 'the arrangement of two things'. This refers to the process of ordering two propositions or concepts to produce a third — essentially, the act of reasoning or inference. This definition is concise: it captures the core operation of logic, where from two givens we derive a new piece of knowledge. In the broader context of the text, this definition of fikr is foundational because logic (mantiq) is concerned with the rules that govern correct thinking. The author is setting up the conceptual framework before delving into the specific forms of argument. Note that the terminological definition is very brief and might be expanded by later commentators, but here it serves as a precise mental hook.

Key Terms
فكر
معقولات
تخيل

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
Fikr is defined linguistically as the soul's movement among intelligibles, and terminologically as the arrangement of two premises – the foundational act of logical inference.

The Linguistic and Technical Definition of Fikr
Bajuri defines fikr linguistically and technically.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the linguistic and technical definitions of fikr as used in Islamic logic.

Core Idea
Fikr is a structured arrangement of knowns to reach an unknown, not mere mental activity.

This passage establishes the foundational definition of fikr (thought). Bajuri contrasts its everyday linguistic sense—movement of the soul among intelligibles—with its precise technical meaning in logic: the ordering of two known matters to attain an unknown. Grasping this distinction is essential because all subsequent logical operations presuppose this technical sense.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Two Definitions
The passage gives two definitions: linguistic (حركة النفس في المعقولات) and technical (ترتيب أمرين معلومين ليتوصل بهما إلى أمر مجهول). Note the contrast between general mental movement and ordered reasoning.
2
Understand the Linguistic Definition
Linguistically, fikr is the movement of the soul among intelligibles (abstract concepts), as opposed to sensibles, which falls under imagination (تخيّل). This highlights that fikr deals with non-sensory objects.
3
Analyse the Technical Definition
Technically, fikr requires arrangement (ترتيب) of two known matters to reach an unknown. This emphasizes order and purpose, distinguishing it from random thought.
4
Note the Scope of the Unknown
The unknown can be either a concept (تصوّري) or an assent (تصديقي). This shows that fikr covers both definition and proof.

وَالفِكْرُ لُغَةً : حَرَكَةُ النَّفْسِ فِي المَعْقُولَاتِ، بِخِلَافِهَا فِي المَحْسُوسَاتِ، فَإِنَّهَا تَخَيُّلٌ. وَاصْطِلَاحاً : تَرْتِيبُ أَمْرَيْنِ مَعْلُومَيْنِ لِيُتَوَصَّلَ بِهِمَا إِلَى أَمْرٍ مَجْهُولٍ؛ تَصَوُّرِيًّا كَانَ، أَوْ تَصْدِيقِيًّا. فَالأَوَّلُ كَمَا فِي قَوْلِكَ فِي تَعْرِيفِ الإِنْسَانِ: «هُوَ : حَيَوَانٌ نَاطِقٌ»، فَإِنَّ فِيهِ تَرْتِيبَ أَمْرَيْنِ مَعْلُومَيْنِ - وَهُمَا : الجِنْسُ وَالفَصْلُ - لِيَتَوَصَّلَ بِهِمَا إِلَى أَمْرٍ مَجْهُولٍ تَصَوُّرِيٍّ - وَهُوَ الإِنْسَانُ -. وَالثَّانِي كَمَا فِي قَوْلِكَ فِي الِاسْتِدْلَالِ عَلَى حُدُوثِ العَالَمِ: «العَالَمُ مُتَغَيِّرٌ، وَكُلُّ مُتَغَيِّرٍ حَادِثٌ»، فَإِنَّ فِيهِ تَرْتِيبَ أَمْرَيْنِ مَعْلُومَيْنِ - وَهُمَا المُقَدِّمَتَانِ المَذْكُورَتَانِ - لِيَتَوَصَّلَ بِهِمَا إِلَى أَمْرٍ مَجْهُولٍ تَصْدِيقِيٍّ - وَهُوَ : «ثُبُوتُ الحُدُوثِ لِلعَالَمِ» -.
And fikr linguistically is: the movement of the soul among intelligibles, unlike its movement among sensibles, for that is imagination. And technically: the arrangement of two known things to reach an unknown thing, whether it be a concept or an assent. The first is like your statement in the definition of 'human': 'He is a rational animal', for it contains arrangement of two known things - which are genus and differentia - to reach an unknown concept - which is the human. And the second is like your statement in the demonstration of the temporal origination of the world: 'The world is changing, and every changing thing is originated', for it contains arrangement of two known things - which are the two mentioned premises - to reach an unknown assent - which is 'the establishment of temporality for the world'.
Source pages p. 63

Commentary
This passage defines the term fikr (thought) from both linguistic and technical perspectives. Linguistically, fikr is the movement of the soul among intelligible concepts, as opposed to its movement among sensible objects, which is called imagination (takhayyul). Technically, in the science of logic, fikr is defined as the ordering of two known matters to arrive at an unknown matter. This unknown result can be either a concept (tasawwur) or an assent (tasdiq). The author then provides two examples. For concept, he gives the definition of 'human' as 'rational animal': the genus 'animal' and the differentia 'rational' are the two known things, and the unknown being defined is the concept of 'human'. For assent, he gives the classical syllogism demonstrating the temporal origin of the world: 'The world is changing; everything changing is originated' — the two premises are known, and the conclusion — that the world is originated — is the unknown assent. This illustrates how logic organizes known information to produce new knowledge, whether that knowledge is a definition (tasawwur) or a proof (tasdiq).

تَصَوُّر
تَصْدِيق

B) Arrangement of two known matters

Fikr in logic is the purposeful ordering of known information to attain new knowledge.

Tasawwur and Tasdiq: The Two Fruits of Fikr
Bajuri gives examples of tasawwuri and tasdiqi results of fikr.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify a given logical product as either a tasawwur or a tasdiq based on the provided examples.

Core Idea
Tasawwur is simple apprehension; tasdiq is affirmation or negation with truth value.

After defining fikr, Bajuri illustrates its two possible outcomes: a concept (tasawwur) or an assent (tasdiq). These two categories are fundamental in Islamic epistemology, and this passage provides canonical examples from definition and demonstration.

Guided Path
1
Recall the Technical Definition of Fikr
Fikr aims at an unknown. That unknown can be either a concept or an assent. This step sets the stage for the examples.
2
Identify the First Example (Tasawwur)
The definition of 'human' as 'rational animal' uses known genus (animal) and differentia (rational) to produce the concept of human. This is a tasawwur because it simply grasps the essence.
3
Identify the Second Example (Tasdiq)
The syllogism 'the world is changing, every changing thing is originated' produces the judgment that the world is originated. This is a tasdiq because it affirms a property of the subject.
4
Compare the Two
Tasawwur is a simple idea; tasdiq is a proposition that can be true or false. Both result from fikr but serve different purposes.

Source Text

Resolving a Logical Objection: Can Non-Existent Ignorance Be Compared to Clouds?
Bajuri defends a poetic simile from a logical objection about existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyze a dialectical objection and response by identifying the logical moves of redefinition and appeal to shared attributes.

Core Idea
To save a comparison, redefine the term or find a shared attribute beyond existence.

The poet compared ignorance to clouds, but an objector objects: ignorance is non-existent while clouds are existent. How can a non-existent be compared to an existent? The commentator Bajuri provides a two-part defense that reveals the precision of logical analysis in Islamic scholarship. In this lesson, we dissect his argument and see how a skilled logician handles such objections.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
The objector claims the comparison is invalid because ignorance (non-existent) and clouds (existent) differ in the fundamental attribute of existence. Find the exact statement of this objection in the Arabic.
The objection is introduced with 'لا يُقَالُ' (it may not be said) but is the position being refuted.
2
Recognize the objection's logical force
The objector's reasoning is that a comparison requires shared attributes, and existence/non-existence is the most basic attribute. Without that, the comparison fails. Why might this seem compelling?
3
Extract the first response: redefinition
Bajuri first responds that the intended 'ignorance' in the poem is compound ignorance (al-jahl al-murakkab), which is an existent mental state. This removes the problem of comparing non-existent to existent.
Compound ignorance is defined as perceiving something contrary to its reality – an actual psychological state.
4
Extract the second response: a general principle
Even if ignorance were simple (non-existent), Bajuri argues that comparison is still valid if the two things share any descriptive attribute, regardless of existence. Identify this principle in the text.
The shared attribute here is 'veiling' (hijab) – both ignorance and clouds veil something.
5
Synthesize the full defense
The commentator provides two independent justifications: (1) the term 'ignorance' refers to compound ignorance, which is existent; (2) even if it were simple, comparison is allowed via a shared attribute. This double-layered reasoning shows thoroughness.
The second justification is more general and shows that existence is not the only relevant property.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لَا يُقَالُ: كَيْفَ يُشَبَّهُ الجَهْلُ بِالسَّحَابِ مَعَ أَنَّ الجَهْلَ عَدَمِيٌّ؛ لِأَنَّهُ: «عَدَمُ العِلْمِ بِالشَّيْء»، وَالسَّحَابَ وُجُودِيٌّ؛ لِأَنَّهُ : أَبْخِرَةٌ تَصَاعَدَتْ وَانْعَقَدَتْ عَلَى مَا قَالَهُ الحُكَمَاءُ، أَوْ ثَمَرُ شَجَرٍ فِي الجَنَّةِ» عَلَى مَا فِي بَعْضِ الآثَارِ الَّتِي نَقَلَهَا فِيهِ السُّيُوطِيُّ فِي كِتَابِ الهَيْئَةِ السَّنِيَّةِ فِي الهَيئَةِ السُّنّيَّةِ، وَجَرَيَانُ التَّشْبِيهِ بَيْنَ عَدَمِيٌّ وَوُجُودِي غَيْرُ سَدِيدٍ؛ لِافْتِرَاقِهِمَا فِي الصِّفَةِ؛ إِذْ صِفَةُ أَحَدِهِمَا العَدَمُ، وَصِفَةُ الآخَرِ الوُجُودُ. لِأَنَّا نَقُولُ: مُرَادُهُ بِـ «الجَهْلِ» هُنَا : «الجَهْلُ المُرَكَّبُ كَمَا أَشَارَ لَهُ فِي شَرْحِهِ»، وَهُوَ وُجُودِيٌّ؛ لِأَنَّهُ : إِدْرَاكُ الشَّيْءِ عَلَى خِلَافِ مَا هُوَ عَلَيْهِ، وَحِينَئِذٍ فَكُلٌّ مِنَ المُشَبَّهِ وَالمُشَبَّهِ وُجُودِيٌّ؛ عَلَى أَنَّهُ لَا مَانِعَ مِنْ تَشْبِيهِ العَدَمِيِّ بِالوُجُودِيِّ، أَوْ عَكْسِهِ إِذَا اشْتَرَكَا فِي وَصْفٍ مِنَ الأَوْصَافِ، وَإِنِ اخْتَلَفَا مِنْ جِهَةِ الوُجُودِ وَالعَدَمِ. نَعَمْ؛ يَتَعَيَّنُ أَنْ يُرَادَ بِـ «الجَهْلِ» هُنَا : الجَهْلُ المُرَكَّبُ لَكِنْ لَا مِنْ جِهَةِ التَّشْبِيهِ، بَلْ مِنْ جِهَةٍ أُخْرَى، وَهِيَ أَنَّهُ هُوَ الَّذِي يُتَعَقَّلُ فِيهِ أَنَّهُ حِجَابٌ، دُونَ الجَهْلِ البَسِيطِ، فَلْيُتَأَمَّلْ.
It may not be said: How is ignorance compared to clouds while ignorance is non-existent, because it is the absence of knowledge of something, and clouds are existent, because they are vapors that rose and coagulated according to the philosophers, or the fruit of a tree in Paradise according to some reports that al-Suyuti transmitted in his book 'al-Hay'a al-Saniyya fi al-Hay'a al-Sunniyya'? And the running of comparison between a non-existent and an existent is not sound, due to their difference in attribute, for the attribute of one is non-existence and the attribute of the other is existence. Because we say: His intent by 'ignorance' here is 'compound ignorance', as he indicated in his commentary, and it is existent, because it is the perception of something contrary to what it is. Then both the compared and the comparator are existent. Moreover, there is no impediment to comparing the non-existent to the existent, or vice versa, when they share a description, even if they differ in terms of existence and non-existence. Yes, it is determined that what is meant by 'ignorance' here is compound ignorance, but not from the aspect of the comparison, rather from another aspect, which is that it is the one in which it can be conceived that it is a veil, unlike simple ignorance. So reflect.
Source pages p. 71

Commentary
This passage presents a dialectical objection and its answer regarding the simile in the poem. The objector argues that ignorance (jahl) is non-existent ('adami), as it is defined as the absence of knowledge, while clouds are existent (wujudi) things. Therefore, comparing a non-existent to an existent is invalid because they differ in the fundamental attribute of existence. The commentator responds by clarifying that the intended meaning of 'ignorance' here is compound ignorance (al-jahl al-murakkab), which is the mistaken belief that one knows something when one does not. This is an existent state of mind, making the comparison valid. Additionally, the commentator argues that even if the ignorance were simple, comparing non-existent to existent is permissible if they share some attribute—here, both veil perception. Finally, the passage adds a refinement: compound ignorance is intended not primarily for the simile but because only it can be conceived as a 'veil' (hijab) that obstructs knowledge, unlike simple ignorance which is mere absence. This multiple-layered response shows careful reasoning typical of the logical commentary tradition.

الجَهْل البَسِيط
الجَهْل المُرَكَّب
تَشْبِيه العَدَمِيِّ بِالوُجُودِيِّ

C) The comparison is allowed if they share a descriptive attribute.
D) The objector's argument is based on a false premise.

Key Takeaway
The validity of a comparison depends not only on the existential status of the things compared but on the relevance of the shared attribute.

Al-Bajuri Defines Compound Ignorance
Author defines the term 'ignorance' as compound ignorance and explains its existential nature.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify compound ignorance and distinguish it from simple ignorance using al-Bajuri's definition and the veil metaphor.

Core Idea
Compound ignorance is a positive false perception, making it existent, unlike simple ignorance which is mere absence.

Al-Bajuri, in his commentary, clarifies a key term in the poem: 'ignorance' does not mean simple lack of knowledge but rather compound ignorance—a false belief. This distinction is crucial for understanding comparisons in logic and theology, where compound ignorance can serve as a basis for analogy despite being false. Let's explore his reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term being defined
In the Arabic, find where al-Bajuri states that 'ignorance' (al-jahl) here means compound ignorance (al-jahl al-murakkab). He signals this by saying 'مُرَادُهُ بِالجَهْلِ هُنَا: الجَهْلُ المُرَكَّبُ' (His intent by 'ignorance' here is compound ignorance).
2
Understand al-Bajuri's definition
Al-Bajuri defines compound ignorance as 'إِدْرَاكُ الشَّيْءِ عَلَى خِلَافِ مَا هُوَ عَلَيْهِ' (perception of a thing contrary to what it is). This is a positive mental state, not an absence. Note that it is a form of perception (idrak), making it existent (wujudi).
The genus is 'perception', the differentia is 'contrary to reality'.
3
Recognize the existential status
Because compound ignorance is a perception, it is an existent (wujudi) entity. Al-Bajuri explicitly says 'وَهُوَ وُجُودِيٌّ' (and it is existent). This contrasts with simple ignorance, which is the mere lack of knowledge and thus non-existent.
This existential status allows compound ignorance to serve as a basis for comparison (tashbih) with other existents.
4
Examine the application: veil metaphor
Al-Bajuri refines the point: compound ignorance is chosen not for comparison but because it is conceptually a veil (hijab) that obstructs knowledge. He says 'أَنَّهُ هُوَ الَّذِي يُتَعَقَّلُ فِيهِ أَنَّهُ حِجَابٌ' (it is that in which it is conceivable that it is a veil). Simple ignorance, being non-existent, cannot function as a veil.
This metaphor is central to understanding why compound ignorance is used in the poem's discussion of comparisons.
5
Contrast with simple ignorance
Simple ignorance (al-jahl al-basit) is the absence of knowledge—non-existent and not a veil. Al-Bajuri emphasizes that the intended meaning here is only compound ignorance, as simple ignorance does not have the property of being a veil.
This contrast ensures you do not confuse the two types.

Source Text

لِأَنَّا نَقُولُ: مُرَادُهُ بِالجَهْلِ هُنَا: الجَهْلُ المُرَكَّبُ كَمَا أَشَارَ لَهُ فِي شَرْحِهِ، وَهُوَ وُجُودِيٌّ؛ لِأَنَّهُ: إِدْرَاكُ الشَّيْءِ عَلَى خِلَافِ مَا هُوَ عَلَيْهِ، وَحِينَئِذٍ فَكُلٌّ مِنَ المُشَبَّهِ وَالمُشَبَّهِ بِهِ وُجُودِيٌّ؛ عَلَى أَنَّهُ لَا مَانِعَ مِنْ تَشْبِيهِ العَدَمِيِّ بِالوُجُودِيِّ، أَوْ عَكْسِهِ إِذَا اشْتَرَكَا فِي وَصْفٍ مِنَ الأَوْصَافِ، وَإِنِ اخْتَلَفَا مِنْ جِهَةِ الوُجُودِ وَالعَدَمِ. نَعَمْ؛ يَتَعَيَّنُ أَنْ يُرَادَ بِالجَهْلِ هُنَا: الجَهْلُ المُرَكَّبُ لَكِنْ لَا مِنْ جِهَةِ التَّشْبِيهِ، بَلْ مِنْ جِهَةٍ أُخْرَى، وَهِيَ أَنَّهُ هُوَ الَّذِي يُتَعَقَّلُ فِيهِ أَنَّهُ حِجَابٌ، دُونَ الجَهْلِ البَسِيطِ، فَلْيُتَأَمَّلْ.
For we say: His intent by 'ignorance' here is compound ignorance, as he indicated in his commentary, and it is existent; because it is perception of a thing contrary to what it is. Then, every likened and likened-to is existent, with the understanding that there is no prevention of likening a non-existent to an existent, or vice versa, when they share in a description, even if they differ in respect of existence and non-existence. Yes, it is determined that by 'ignorance' here is meant compound ignorance, but not from the aspect of likening, rather from another aspect, which is that it is that in which it is conceivable that it is a veil, unlike simple ignorance, so reflect.
Source pages p. 72

Commentary
This passage is a definitional expansion by al-Bajuri. He opens by stating that the 'ignorance' referred to in the poem is compound ignorance (al-jahl al-murakkab), as indicated in his own commentary on the poem. He then defines compound ignorance as a perception of something contrary to its true reality, which makes it an existent (wujudi) entity, not a mere absence. Because it is a positive cognitive state, it can serve as the basis for comparisons (tashbih). He clarifies that likening a non-existent to an existent or vice versa is permissible if they share a description, even if they differ in ontological status. He then refines the point: the compound ignorance intended here is not chosen for the purpose of likening, but because it is conceptually a veil (hijab) that obstructs knowledge, unlike simple ignorance which is non-existent. Thus the focus is on the veil metaphor.

Key Terms
الجَهْلُ المُرَكَّبُ
الجَهْلُ البَسِيطُ

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because it is a real entity like a physical object.
B) Because it is a perception of a thing contrary to reality.
C) Because it is the absence of knowledge.
D) Because it can be seen.

Key Takeaway
Compound ignorance is existent because it is a positive false perception, and it functions as a veil obscuring truth, unlike simple ignorance which is mere absence.

Al-Anbabii on Compound Ignorance as a Convention
Commentator defends the definition against objections about composition from simple ignorance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why 'compound ignorance' is not a real composite and refute objections to its definition.

Core Idea
Compound ignorance is called 'compound' by convention, not because it actually has parts.

In this passage, al-Anbabii responds to objections about the definition of compound ignorance. The objector argues that if it is composite, its parts must be impossible. Al-Anbabii shows that 'compound' is merely a conventional label, not a literal composition. Understanding this resolves the paradox and clarifies the concept.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
The objector asks: if compound ignorance is composite, what are its parts? They cannot be knowledge (opposite), nor compound ignorance (regress), nor simple ignorance (non-existent). This implies the definition is flawed.
This objection stems from taking 'compound' literally.
2
Locate the key phrase 'mujarrad istilah'
Al-Anbabii states 'tusammiyatuhu murakkaban mahd istilah' – calling it compound is pure convention. This is the central claim.
Istilah means technical term, not a description of reality.
3
Understand the alternative answer
Another answer: compound ignorance entails two simple ignorances: not knowing the thing and not knowing one's ignorance. This shows 'compound' means 'entailing multiple elements', not literally composed.
This satisfies the intuition of composition without actual parts.
4
Apply this to avoid false objections
Recognize that technical terms in Islamic sciences often have conventional meanings. When a term seems to imply an impossible composition, check if it is a convention.
This prevents falling into spurious paradoxes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (لِأَنَّا نَقُولُ: مُرَادُهُ بِالجَهْلِ هُنَا: الجَهْلُ المُرَكَّبُ) قد يقال: حينئذ لا يتسبب على زوال الجهل المركب إخراج النتائج، ولا يتفرع عليه بدو شموس المعارف؛ إذ زوال الجهل المركب يتحقق مع وجود الجهل البسيط، فيعكر على ما سبق وعلى ما يأتي. إلا أن يقال: إن قرينة المدح قاضية بأن المراد إزالة الجهل المركب بإثبات العلم الذي هو ضده، فحينئذ لا يرد هذا البحث؛ تأمل. ثم إن تسميته مركباً محض اصطلاح، وإلا ففي الحقيقة لا تركيب؛ لأنه اعتقاد، والاعتقاد بسيط. وبهذا يندفع استشكال كثير من القاصرين لذلك؛ بأنه إذا كان مركباً فلا يخلو: إما أن تكون أجزاؤه التي تركَّب منها من قبيل العلم، أو الجهل: - لا جائز أن تكون من الأول؛ لأن الشيء لا يتركب من ضده. - ولا أن تكون من الثاني؛ لأن أجزاءه لو كانت من قبيل الجهل المركب، نقل الكلام إليها، ويلزم التسلسل. أو من قبيل الجهل البسيط، فالجهل البسيط مفهومه عدمي، والوجودي لا تكون أجزاؤه عدمية؛ إذ لا يتركب الوجودي من العدمي، فمن أي شيء تركَّب؟ وحاصل الجواب: أن هذه الشبهة مبناها توهم أنه مركب حقيقةً كتركيب السرير من أجزائه، وهذا غير مراد، بل التسمية بذلك مجرد اصطلاح خال عن المناسبة. وقد يجاب أيضاً: بأن معنى كونه مركباً أنه مستلزم لجهلين بسيطين: عدم العلم بالشيء، وعدم العلم بأنه جاهل؛ تأمل.
His statement: (For we say: His intent by 'ignorance' here is compound ignorance.) It may be said: Then the extraction of conclusions does not result from the removal of compound ignorance, nor does the appearance of the suns of knowledge branch from it; because the removal of compound ignorance occurs while simple ignorance remains, so it contradicts what came before and what will come. Unless it is said: The context of praise indicates that what is intended is the removal of compound ignorance by establishing knowledge which is its opposite, then this line of inquiry does not arise; reflect. Then, calling it 'compound' is pure convention; otherwise, in reality there is no composition, because it is a belief, and belief is simple. And by this, the objection of many of the incapable is repelled: that if it is compound, it must be that its parts are either from knowledge or from ignorance. It is not possible that they are from the first, because a thing is not composed of its opposite. Nor that they are from the second, because if its parts were from compound ignorance, the discussion would transfer to them, leading to regress. Or from simple ignorance, but simple ignorance is non-existent, and an existent cannot have non-existent parts, so of what is it composed? The summary of the answer: This sophism is based on the illusion that it is literally composite like the composition of a bed from its parts, and this is not intended; rather, the naming is a mere convention devoid of appropriateness. It may also be answered: That the meaning of its being compound is that it entails two simple ignorances: not knowing the thing, and not knowing that one is ignorant; reflect.
Source pages p. 72

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement by al-Anbabii, addressing objections to al-Bajuri's definition of compound ignorance. The objection claims that removing compound ignorance does not necessarily produce knowledge because simple ignorance remains, so the poem's claim of resulting knowledge is undermined. Al-Anbabii responds that the praise context shows the intent is to replace compound ignorance with its opposite, knowledge. He then tackles a deeper objection: if compound ignorance is composite, what are its parts? They cannot be knowledge (opposite), nor compound ignorance (regress), nor simple ignorance (non-existent). The answer is that 'compound' is a mere terminological convention (istilah), not literal composition. In reality, it is a simple belief. The true meaning is that it entails two simple ignorances: ignorance of the fact and ignorance of one's ignorance. This resolves the paradox.

اصطلاح
تسلسل

A) The parts are simple knowledge.
B) The term 'compound' is just a convention, not literal.
C) Compound ignorance has no parts.
D) Simple ignorance is composite.

Key Takeaway
Compound ignorance is a conventional label for a simple belief that entails two lacks of knowledge; it is not a literal composite.

Refuting the Claim that Simple Ignorance is Composite
Al-Anbabi refutes the misconception that simple ignorance is composed

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between real composition and conventional terminology in the concept of simple ignorance.

Core Idea
Simple ignorance is a negative concept; calling it 'composed' is a convention, not a real part-whole structure.

In this passage, al-Anbabi addresses a common confusion: some mistakenly think simple ignorance (al-jahl al-basit) is composed of parts, like a physical object. He clarifies that simple ignorance is a negative concept, and the term 'composition' here is merely a conventional label. Understanding this distinction protects you from misinterpreting logical terminology.

Guided Path
1
Identify the target claim
Find where the author states the erroneous view: 'that it is truly composed like the composition of a bed from its parts'.
This is the claim being refuted.
2
Understand the principle of composition
The author states 'the existent cannot have its parts be non-existent'. This is a logical principle: a positive thing cannot be composed of negatives.
Simple ignorance is non-existent, so it cannot be a real composite.
3
Distinguish real vs. conventional composition
The author clarifies that the term 'composed' for simple ignorance is a mere convention, not real composition.
The passage says 'the naming by that is merely a convention devoid of relevance'.
4
Learn the alternative interpretation
The author offers an alternative: that 'composition' means simple ignorance entails two lacks: lack of knowledge and lack of awareness of ignorance.
This shows it is a logical entailment, not a part-whole relation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أو من قبيل الجهل البسيط، فالجهلُ البسيط مفهومه عدميّ، والوجودي لا تكون أجزاؤه عدميَّةٌ ; إذ لا يتركَّب الوجودي مِنَ العدمي، فمن أي شيء تركَّب؟ وحاصل الجواب : أنَّ هذه الشُّبهة مبناها تَوهُم أَنَّه مركَّب حقيقةً كـ: «تركب السرير من أجزائه»، وهذا غير مراد، بلِ التَّسمية بذلك مجرَّدُ اصطلاح خال عن المناسبة. وقد يجاب أيضاً : بأنَّ معنى كونه مركَّباً أنَّه مستلزم لجهلين بسيطين : عدم العلم بالشَّيء، وعدم العِلم بأنَّه جاهل ; تأمَّل .
Or it is from the category of simple ignorance. For simple ignorance its concept is non-existent ('adami), and the existent (wujudi) cannot have its parts be non-existent, since the existent is not composed from the non-existent. So from what is it composed? The gist of the answer: that this sophism is based on the illusion that it is truly composed like the composition of a bed from its parts, and this is not intended; rather the naming by that is merely a convention devoid of relevance. And it may also be answered: that the meaning of it being composed is that it entails two simple ignorances: lack of knowledge of the thing, and lack of knowledge that one is ignorant; reflect.
Source pages p. 73

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement by al-Anbabi addressing the claim that simple ignorance (al-jahl al-basit) is a composite concept. Al-Anbabi begins by stating that the concept of simple ignorance is non-existent ('adami) — it is the absence of knowledge. A key principle is introduced: an existent thing (wujudi) cannot have its parts be non-existent, because a positive thing cannot be composed of negatives. The sophism (shubha) is that some might consider simple ignorance as composed of parts, like a bed is composed of its parts. Al-Anbabi refutes this by clarifying that the term 'composition' here is merely a conventional usage, not a real composition. He offers an alternative answer: if we say it is composed, it means it entails two simple ignorances: (1) lack of knowledge of the thing, and (2) lack of knowledge that one is ignorant. This shows that 'composition' here indicates a logical entailment, not a physical or metaphysical part-whole relation. The lesson guards against confusing conventional terminology with real composition. In Islamic logic, this is important for distinguishing between types of ignorance: simple ignorance (not knowing and not knowing that one does not know) versus compound ignorance (not knowing but thinking one knows). The example can be seen in theology: someone ignorant of the attributes of Allah who is unaware of his ignorance has simple ignorance; it is not composed of separate components but is a single absence.

الوجودي
العدم
مركب

D) It does not exist at all.

Key Takeaway
Simple ignorance is a single negative concept; its 'composition' is only conventional, denoting logical entailment, not real parts.

Understanding Takhsis and Qasr in the Praise of the Prophet
Commentary explains takhsis and qasr in the poem's opening.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the maqsūr and maqsūr ʿalayh in a takhsis construction and explain the role of the particle ba'.

Core Idea
Takhsis restricts a meaning to a specific referent using a verb, a maqsūr, a maqsūr ʿalayh, and an optional ba'.

In the opening line of al-Sullam al-Murawniq, the poet praises the Prophet using a takhsis construction. The commentary explains that the verb 'khassa' is used with a maqsūr and a maqsūr ʿalayh. Understanding this structure is key to interpreting the line correctly and appreciating the rhetorical power of qasr in Islamic texts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the takhsis verb
Look for 'khassa' or similar in the Arabic. In the poem, 'من خصنا' indicates the verb of specification.
2
Find the maqsūr
The thing restricted: 'نا' (us, the Muslims) is the maqsūr, the object of the specification.
3
Find the maqsūr ʿalayh
The thing to which it is restricted: 'بخير من أرسلا' (with the best of messengers). The particle 'ba' introduces it.
The ba' can optionally attach to the maqsūr or maqsūr ʿalayh, as discussed later.
4
Understand the optionality of ba'
The commentary states that after a takhsis verb, a maqsūr, a maqsūr ʿalayh, and a ba' are required, but the ba' enters optionally on one of them, by agreement of al-Sa'd and al-Sayyid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَإِلَّا فَأَصْلُ الإِيمَانِ وَأَصْلُ الإِسْلَامِ لَا تَلَازُمَ بَيْنَهُمَا وُجُوداً حَتَّى يَتَّحِدَا مَحَلَّا ، بَلْ قَدْ يَنْفَرِدُ الإِيمَانُ كَمَا فِي المُصَدِّقِ بِقَلْبِهِ غَيْرِ المُنْقَادِ، وَقَدْ يَنْفَرِدُ الإِسْلَامُ كَمَا فِي المُنْقَادِ غَيْرِ المُصَدِّقِ بِقَلْبِهِ . وَلَمَّا كَانَتْ نِعْمَةُ الإِيمَانِ وَنِعْمَةُ الإِسْلَامِ أَجَلَّ النِّعَمِ وَأَسَاسَهَا، خَصَّهَا المُصَنِّفُ بِالذِّكْرِ . قَوْلُهُ : (مَنْ خَصَّنَا ... إلخ) خَبَرٌ لِمُبْتَدَأَ مَحْذُوفٍ . وَالجُمْلَةُ مُسْتَأنَفَةٌ اسْتِثْنَافاً بَيَانِيا ؛ لِأَنَّهَا سِيقَتْ فِي جَوَابِ سُؤَالٍ ؛ تَقْدِيرُهُ: «مَنِ المَحْمُودُ؟» . وَاعْلَمْ أَنَّهُ لَا بُدَّ بَعْدَ مَادَّةِ التَّخْصِيصِ وَنَحْوِهَا مِنْ : مَقْصُورٍ، وَمَقْصُورٍ عَلَيْهِ، وَبَاءِ تَدْخُلُ عَلَى أَحَدِهِمَا جَوَازاً بِاتِّفَاقِ كُلِّ مِنَ السَّعْدِ وَالسَّيِّدِ، وَإِنْ كَانَ الغَالِبُ عِنْدَ السَّعْدِ دُخُولُهَا عَلَى المَقْصُورِ، وَعِنْدَ السَّيِّدِ دُخُولُهَا عَلَى المَقْصُورِ عَلَيْهِ؛ كَذَا قَالَ ابْنُ قَاسِم . وَقَدْ رَدَّهُ الشَّيْخُ يَاسِينُ بِأَنَّ السَّيِّدَ صَرَّحَ بِمَا قَالَهُ السَّعْدُ فِي حَاشِيَةِ المُطَوَّلِ وَغَيْرِهَا، وَبِذَلِكَ تَعْلَمُ مَا فِي الصَّابِطِ المَشْهُورِ، وَهُوَ قَوْلُ بَعْضِهِمْ : وَالبَاءُ بَعْدَ الِاخْتِصَاصِ يَكْفُرُ دُخُولُهَا عَلَى الَّذِي قَدْ قَصَرُوا . وَيُجَابُ عَنِ الضَّابِطِ بِأَنَّهُ جَرَى فِيهِ عَلَى مَا نَقَلَهُ يَاسِينُ؛ غَايَتُهُ أَنَّ فِيهِ اكْتِفَاءً، فَقَوْلُهُ : ذَكَرَهُ الحَبْرُ الهُمَامُ السَّيِّدُ أَيْ : وَالسَّعْدُ أَيْضاً، وَإِنَّمَا اكْتَفَى بِالسَّيِّدِ عَنِ السَّعْدِ وَلَمْ يَعْكِسْ؛ لِأَنَّ نِسْبَةَ مَا ذُكِرَ لِلسَّعْدِ شَهِيرَةٌ، بِخِلَافِ نِسْبَتِهِ لِلسَّيِّدِ، فَلَمَّا لَمْ تَكُنْ شَهِيرَةً نَبَّهَ عَلَيْهَا؛ لِأَنَّهَا مَحَلُّ التَّوَهُمِ.
Otherwise, the essence of faith and the essence of Islam are not necessarily connected in existence such that they become one entity; rather, faith may exist alone as in the one who believes in his heart but does not submit, and Islam may exist alone as in the one who submits but does not believe in his heart. And since the blessing of faith and the blessing of Islam are the greatest blessings and their foundation, the author mentioned them specifically. His statement: 'Who distinguished us... etc.' is the predicate of an omitted subject. The sentence is a resumptive explanatory one because it is given as an answer to a question, the implied question being: 'Who is the praised one?' And know that after the particle of takhsis and the like, there must be a maqsūr, a maqsūr ʿalayh, and a bā' that enters optionally on one of them, by the agreement of both al-Saʿd and al-Sayyid, although the prevalent usage according to al-Saʿd is that it enters on the maqsūr, and according to al-Sayyid that it enters on the maqsūr ʿalayh; thus said Ibn Qāsim. And Shaykh Yāsīn refuted this by stating that al-Sayyid explicitly agreed with what al-Saʿd said in his gloss on al-Muṭawwal and elsewhere, and by that you know what is wrong with the well-known verse, which is the statement of some: 'The bā' after takhsīs/k sometimes causes disbelief, its entry on that which they have restricted.' And the verse is answered by saying that it follows what Yāsīn transmitted; the point is that it involves economy, so his statement 'The erudite scholar al-Sayyid mentioned it' means: and al-Saʿd also. He only sufficed with al-Sayyid over al-Saʿd and did not reverse because the attribution of what was mentioned to al-Saʿd is well-known, unlike its attribution to al-Sayyid. So since it was not well-known, he drew attention to it because it is a place of misconception.
Source pages p. 86

Commentary
This passage is a commentary from a hashiya on the opening line of al-Sullam al-Murawniq, which praises the Prophet (صلى الله عليه وسلم). The commentator begins by explaining the meaning of the line, focusing on the distinction between faith (iman) and Islam. He clarifies that they are not necessarily always together; a person may have inward belief without outward submission (munqad), and vice versa. The author (of the poem) specifically mentions these two blessings because they are the greatest. Then the commentary moves to a grammatical/logical analysis of the phrase 'Who distinguished us with the best of those who were sent...' The commentator explains that the sentence is a predicate of an omitted subject, answering an implied question 'Who is the praised one?' This is a rhetorical device for emphasis.

Next, the commentator discusses the structure known as 'takhsis' (specification) and its related terms: maqsūr (the thing restricted) and maqsūr ʿalayh (the thing to which it is restricted). He explains that after a word indicating takhsis (like the verb 'khassa') or similar verbs (like 'akhassa', 'tamayyaza', etc.), there must be a particle 'ba' that optionally enters either the maqsūr or the maqsūr ʿalayh. This is agreed upon by two scholars, al-Sa'd (al-Taftazani) and al-Sayyid (al-Sharif al-Jurjani), though they differed on which is more common: al-Sa'd held that 'ba' normally enters the maqsūr, while al-Sayyid held it enters the maqsūr ʿalayh. The commentator then cites Ibn Qasim's transmission of this difference.

However, a later scholar, Shaykh Yasin, rejected Ibn Qasim's account, asserting that al-Sayyid in fact explicitly agreed with al-Sa'd in his gloss on al-Mutawwal. This leads to a discussion of a well-known poetic verse (a 'dabit' or mnemonic) that says: 'The ba after takhsis/kuffar? kafara, its entry on that which they have restricted...' The commentator examines this verse and explains that it is not actually incorrect, but rather it follows Yasin's transmission and uses economy of expression: it mentions only al-Sayyid but implies al-Sa'd as well, because al-Sa'd's position is well-known and al-Sayyid's is less known and thus needs emphasis. Thus the apparent contradiction between the verse and the scholars' actual positions is resolved. This is a typical refinements of a hashiya where the super-commentator clarifies a point of disagreement and shows that the mnemonic is valid when properly understood.

Key Terms
التخصيص
المقصور
المقصور عليه
الباء

Checkpoint

A) Maqsūr, maqsūr ʿalayh, and ba'
B) Maqsūr, maqsūr ʿalayh, and a verb
C) Maqsūr, ba', and a preposition
D) Maqsūr ʿalayh, ba', and a noun

Key Takeaway
Takhsis is a rhetorical device that restricts a thing to a specific referent using a verb like khassa, a maqsūr, a maqsūr ʿalayh, and an optional particle ba'.

The Dispute Between al-Sa'd and al-Sayyid on the Particle Ba' in Takhsis
Commentary refines understanding of ba' in takhsis.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to trace the disagreement between al-Sa'd and al-Sayyid regarding the particle ba' in takhsis and evaluate Shaykh Yasin's refutation.

Core Idea
The disagreement about ba' is on prevalence, not necessity; Shaykh Yasin shows al-Sayyid agreed with al-Sa'd.

In the hashiya on al-Sullam al-Murawniq, the commentator reports a disagreement between al-Sa'd and al-Sayyid about which element the particle ba' attaches to in a takhsis construction. However, Shaykh Yasin refutes this transmission, claiming al-Sayyid actually agreed with al-Sa'd. This leads to a reconciliation of a famous mnemonic verse. This lesson explores the nuance of scholarly disagreement and how later commentators refine our understanding.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two positions
According to Ibn Qasim, al-Sa'd holds that ba' normally enters the maqsūr, while al-Sayyid holds it normally enters the maqsūr ʿalayh.
2
Understand the refutation by Shaykh Yasin
Shaykh Yasin argues that al-Sayyid explicitly stated agreement with al-Sa'd in his gloss on al-Mutawwal, so the reported disagreement is incorrect.
3
Analyze the mnemonic verse
The verse 'والباء بعد الاختصاص يكفر دخولها على الذي قد قصروا' is interpreted as having economy: it mentions only al-Sayyid but implies al-Sa'd because al-Sa'd's position is well-known.
The word 'يكفر' might be a corruption; the commentary clarifies the verse is not wrong but requires understanding of its conciseness.
4
Conclude the resolution
Both scholars actually agree that the ba' is optional; the dispute is only about which usage is more frequent, and al-Sayyid's view is less known, hence the verse draws attention to it.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَإِلَّا فَأَصْلُ الإِيمَانِ وَأَصْلُ الإِسْلَامِ لَا تَلَازُمَ بَيْنَهُمَا وُجُوداً حَتَّى يَتَّحِدَا مَحَلَّا ، بَلْ قَدْ يَنْفَرِدُ الإِيمَانُ كَمَا فِي المُصَدِّقِ بِقَلْبِهِ غَيْرِ المُنْقَادِ، وَقَدْ يَنْفَرِدُ الإِسْلَامُ كَمَا فِي المُنْقَادِ غَيْرِ المُصَدِّقِ بِقَلْبِهِ . وَلَمَّا كَانَتْ نِعْمَةُ الإِيمَانِ وَنِعْمَةُ الإِسْلَامِ أَجَلَّ النِّعَمِ وَأَسَاسَهَا، خَصَّهَا المُصَنِّفُ بِالذِّكْرِ . قَوْلُهُ : (مَنْ خَصَّنَا ... إلخ) خَبَرٌ لِمُبْتَدَأَ مَحْذُوفٍ . وَالجُمْلَةُ مُسْتَأنَفَةٌ اسْتِثْنَافاً بَيَانِيا ؛ لِأَنَّهَا سِيقَتْ فِي جَوَابِ سُؤَالٍ ؛ تَقْدِيرُهُ: «مَنِ المَحْمُودُ؟» . وَاعْلَمْ أَنَّهُ لَا بُدَّ بَعْدَ مَادَّةِ التَّخْصِيصِ وَنَحْوِهَا مِنْ : مَقْصُورٍ، وَمَقْصُورٍ عَلَيْهِ، وَبَاءِ تَدْخُلُ عَلَى أَحَدِهِمَا جَوَازاً بِاتِّفَاقِ كُلِّ مِنَ السَّعْدِ وَالسَّيِّدِ، وَإِنْ كَانَ الغَالِبُ عِنْدَ السَّعْدِ دُخُولُهَا عَلَى المَقْصُورِ، وَعِنْدَ السَّيِّدِ دُخُولُهَا عَلَى المَقْصُورِ عَلَيْهِ؛ كَذَا قَالَ ابْنُ قَاسِم . وَقَدْ رَدَّهُ الشَّيْخُ يَاسِينُ بِأَنَّ السَّيِّدَ صَرَّحَ بِمَا قَالَهُ السَّعْدُ فِي حَاشِيَةِ المُطَوَّلِ وَغَيْرِهَا، وَبِذَلِكَ تَعْلَمُ مَا فِي الصَّابِطِ المَشْهُورِ، وَهُوَ قَوْلُ بَعْضِهِمْ : وَالبَاءُ بَعْدَ الِاخْتِصَاصِ يَكْفُرُ دُخُولُهَا عَلَى الَّذِي قَدْ قَصَرُوا . وَيُجَابُ عَنِ الضَّابِطِ بِأَنَّهُ جَرَى فِيهِ عَلَى مَا نَقَلَهُ يَاسِينُ؛ غَايَتُهُ أَنَّ فِيهِ اكْتِفَاءً، فَقَوْلُهُ : ذَكَرَهُ الحَبْرُ الهُمَامُ السَّيِّدُ أَيْ : وَالسَّعْدُ أَيْضاً، وَإِنَّمَا اكْتَفَى بِالسَّيِّدِ عَنِ السَّعْدِ وَلَمْ يَعْكِسْ؛ لِأَنَّ نِسْبَةَ مَا ذُكِرَ لِلسَّعْدِ شَهِيرَةٌ، بِخِلَافِ نِسْبَتِهِ لِلسَّيِّدِ، فَلَمَّا لَمْ تَكُنْ شَهِيرَةً نَبَّهَ عَلَيْهَا؛ لِأَنَّهَا مَحَلُّ التَّوَهُمِ.
Otherwise, the essence of faith and the essence of Islam are not necessarily connected in existence such that they become one entity; rather, faith may exist alone as in the one who believes in his heart but does not submit, and Islam may exist alone as in the one who submits but does not believe in his heart. And since the blessing of faith and the blessing of Islam are the greatest blessings and their foundation, the author mentioned them specifically. His statement: 'Who distinguished us... etc.' is the predicate of an omitted subject. The sentence is a resumptive explanatory one because it is given as an answer to a question, the implied question being: 'Who is the praised one?' And know that after the particle of takhsis and the like, there must be a maqsūr, a maqsūr ʿalayh, and a bā' that enters optionally on one of them, by the agreement of both al-Saʿd and al-Sayyid, although the prevalent usage according to al-Saʿd is that it enters on the maqsūr, and according to al-Sayyid that it enters on the maqsūr ʿalayh; thus said Ibn Qāsim. And Shaykh Yāsīn refuted this by stating that al-Sayyid explicitly agreed with what al-Saʿd said in his gloss on al-Muṭawwal and elsewhere, and by that you know what is wrong with the well-known verse, which is the statement of some: 'The bā' after takhsīs/k sometimes causes disbelief, its entry on that which they have restricted.' And the verse is answered by saying that it follows what Yāsīn transmitted; the point is that it involves economy, so his statement 'The erudite scholar al-Sayyid mentioned it' means: and al-Saʿd also. He only sufficed with al-Sayyid over al-Saʿd and did not reverse because the attribution of what was mentioned to al-Saʿd is well-known, unlike its attribution to al-Sayyid. So since it was not well-known, he drew attention to it because it is a place of misconception.

Commentary
This passage is a commentary from a hashiya on the opening line of al-Sullam al-Murawniq, which praises the Prophet (صلى الله عليه وسلم). The commentator begins by explaining the meaning of the line, focusing on the distinction between faith (iman) and Islam. He clarifies that they are not necessarily always together; a person may have inward belief without outward submission (munqad), and vice versa. The author (of the poem) specifically mentions these two blessings because they are the greatest. Then the commentary moves to a grammatical/logical analysis of the phrase 'Who distinguished us with the best of those who were sent...' The commentator explains that the sentence is a predicate of an omitted subject, answering an implied question 'Who is the praised one?' This is a rhetorical device for emphasis.

Next, the commentator discusses the structure known as 'takhsis' (specification) and its related terms: maqsūr (the thing restricted) and maqsūr ʿalayh (the thing to which it is restricted). He explains that after a word indicating takhsis (like the verb 'khassa') or similar verbs (like 'akhassa', 'tamayyaza', etc.), there must be a particle 'ba' that optionally enters either the maqsūr or the maqsūr ʿalayh. The commentator then cites Ibn Qasim's transmission of this difference.

However, a later scholar, Shaykh Yasin, rejected Ibn Qasim's account, asserting that al-Sayyid in fact explicitly agreed with al-Sa'd in his gloss on al-Mutawwal. This leads to a discussion of a well-known poetic verse (a 'dabit' or mnemonic) that says: 'The ba after takhsis/kuffar? kafara, its entry on that which they have restricted...' The commentator examines this verse and explains that it is not actually incorrect, but rather it follows Yasin's transmission and uses economy of expression: it mentions only al-Sayyid but implies al-Sa'd as well, because al-Sa'd's position is well-known and al-Sayyid's is less known and thus needs emphasis. Thus the apparent contradiction between the verse and the scholars' actual positions is resolved. This is a typical refinements of a hashiya where the super-commentator clarifies a point of disagreement and shows that the mnemonic is valid when properly understood.

The 'Ba' in Qasr: Resolving the Scope of the Prophet's Mission
Commentary on the poem's line discussing 'ba' in qasr and the Prophet's universal mission.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two different logical interpretations of the 'ba' in an ikhtisas phrase and determine which element (maqsūr or maqsūr 'alayh) the 'ba' enters upon according to different scholars.

Core Idea
The same 'ba' can indicate restriction to the subject or the object, and the choice changes the theological implication.

The poem line 'khasşana bi-khayri man ursila' (He specified us with the best of those sent) is a classic example of qasr (restriction) in logic and Arabic grammar. The key is the preposition 'ba'—does it enter on the maqsūr (the restricted object) or the maqsūr 'alayh (the one to which restriction is made)? Al-Sa'd and al-Sayyid disagree on the predominant usage, and resolving this disagreement has implications for understanding the universality of the Prophet's mission. In this lesson, we will dissect the commentary to grasp how scholars reconciled the apparent conflict with the established belief that the Prophet was sent to all nations.

Guided Path
1
Identify the source of disagreement
Locate the disagreement between al-Sa'd and al-Sayyid regarding which element the 'ba' enters after ikhtisas. Note the predominant view of each scholar as reported by Ibn Qasim and the subsequent refutation by Shaykh Yasin.
The disagreement is not absolute; both agree on permissibility but differ on typical usage.
2
Examine the famous rule (al-ḍābiṭ)
Read the rule: 'Al-ba'u baʿda al-ikhtiṣāṣi yakfuru dukhūluhā ʿalā alladhī qad qaṣarū.' Understand that it is an abbreviation, and the commentator explains that it includes both al-Sa'd and al-Sayyid, but only al-Sayyid is mentioned because his view is less known.
3
Analyze the apparent theological implication
The poem line seems to imply that the Prophet is restricted only to his nation. Consider why this is problematic given the Islamic belief that he was sent to all nations. The commentator offers two answers based on the interpretation of the 'ba'.
4
Evaluate the two answers
First answer: the 'ba' enters on the maqsūr 'alayh, meaning Allah restricted us to the Prophet (we do not go to other messengers). Second answer: the 'ba' enters on the maqsūr, but the meaning is that He specified us with direct sending, while other nations received the Prophet's message through intermediaries. Decide which answer is considered best and why.
The first answer is deemed best in the passage.
5
Reject an invalid grammatical interpretation
Consider why a proposed interpretation (that the rule's pronoun refers to the maqsūr 'alayh) is invalid due to mismatch of the objects of the two prepositions. This reinforces the technical precision required in such analyses.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مَنْ خَصَّنَا بِخَيْرِ مَنْ قَدْ أُرْسِلَا وَخَيْرِ مَنْ حَازَ المَقَامَاتِ العُلَا
وَنَحْوِهَا مِنْ : مَقْصُورٍ، وَمَقْصُورٍ عَلَيْهِ، وَبَاءِ تَدْخُلُ عَلَى أَحَدِهِمَا جَوَازاً بِاتِّفَاقِ كُلِّ مِنَ السَّعْدِ وَالسَّيِّدِ، وَإِنْ كَانَ الغَالِبُ عِنْدَ السَّعْدِ دُخُولُهَا عَلَى المَقْصُورِ، وَعِنْدَ السَّيِّدِ دُخُولُهَا عَلَى المَقْصُورِ عَلَيْهِ؛ كَذَا قَالَ ابْنُ قَاسِم . وَقَدْ رَدَّهُ الشَّيْخُ يَاسِينُ بِأَنَّ السَّيِّدَ صَرَّحَ بِمَا قَالَهُ السَّعْدُ فِي حَاشِيَةِ المُطَوَّلِ وَغَيْرِهَا، وَبِذَلِكَ تَعْلَمُ مَا فِي الصَّابِطِ المَشْهُورِ، وَهُوَ قَوْلُ بَعْضِهِمْ :
وَالبَاءُ بَعْدَ الِاخْتِصَاصِ يَكْفُرُ دُخُولُهَا عَلَى الَّذِي قَدْ قَصَرُوا
وقوله : (وَنَحْوِهَا) كمادَّة الاختصاص الذي هو مصدر «اختص» والخصوص والتمييز والإفراد، بخلاف مادة القصر، فتتعدى بـ «علی»
وأمَّا التَّعبير بـ «المقصور، والمقصور عليه بعد مادَّة التَّخصيص ،ونحوها، فلبيان المعنى، وإن اختلف المادتان في التَّعدِّي.
قوله : (مَا فِي الضَّابِطِ) أي: مِن أنَّه لم يوافق ما نقله سم، ولا ما نقله اهـ مؤلّف . ويُجاب عنِ الضابط : بأنه جرى فيه على ما نقله يس؛ غايتُهُ أنَّ فيه اكتفاء، فقوله : ذَكَرَهُ الحَبْرُ الهُمَامُ السَّيْدُ أي : والسَّعد أيضاً وإنَّما اكتفى بالسَّيِّد عنِ السَّعد ولم يعكس؛ لأنَّ نسبة ما ذكر للسعد شهيرة، بخلاف نسبته للسَّيِّد، فلما لم تكن شهيرةً نبه عليها؛ لأنَّها محلُّ التَّوهم.
وَعَكْسُهُ مُسْتَعْمَلٌ وَجَيِّدُ ذَكَرَهُ الحَبْرُ الإِمَامُ السَّيِّدُ إِذَا عَلِمْتَ ذَلِكَ عَلِمْتَ أَنَّ المُتَبَادَرَ مِنْ كَلَام المُصَنِّفِ دُخُولُهَا عَلَى المَقْصُورِ كَمَا هُوَ الغَالِبُ عَلَى مَا مَرَّ ، فَمُقْتَضَاهُ أَنَّهُ وَ مَقْصُورٌ عَلَيْنَا لَا يَتَعَدَّانَا إِلَى غَيْرِنَا ، وَلَيْسَ كَذَلِكَ ؛ لِأَنَّ الحَقَّ أَنَّهُ مُرْسَلٌ لِلأُمَمِ السَّابِقَةِ ؛ غَايَةُ الأَمْرِ أَنَّ الرُّسُلَ نُوَّابٌ عَنْهُ ، كَمَا يُشِيرُ لِذَلِكَ قَوْلُ صَاحِبِ البُرْدَةِ :
[ من البسيط]
فَإِنَّهُ شَمْسُ فَضْلِ هُمْ كَوَاكِبُهَا يُظْهِرْنَ أَنْوَارَهَا لِلنَّاسِ فِي الظُّلَمِ
وَأُجِيْبَ عَنْهُ بِأَجْوِبَةٍ ؛ أَحْسَنُهَا : أَنَّ البَاءَ هُنَا دَاخِلَةٌ عَلَى المَقْصُورِ عَلَيْهِ، وَإِنْ كَانَ خِلَافَ الغَالِبِ عَلَى مَا تَقَدَّمَ، وَالمَعْنَى عَلَيْهِ : أَنَّ اللهَ تَعَالَى قَصَرَنَا عَلَيْهِ ، لَا نَتَجَاوَزُهُ إِلَى غَيْرِهِ مِنَ الرُّسُلِ.
أَوْ أَنَّهَا دَاخِلَةٌ عَلَى المَقْصُورِ كَمَا هُوَ الغَالِبُ، لَكِنَّ المُرَادَ أَنَّ اللهَ تَعَالَى خَصَّنَا بِهِ صلى الله عليه وسلم مِنْ حَيْثُ إِرْسَالُهُ لَنَا بِطَرِيقِ المُبَاشَرَةِ، فَلَا يُنَافِي أَنَّهُ أُرْسِلَ أَيْضاً لِغَيْرِنَا مِنَ الْأُمَمِ لَكِنْ بِوَاسِطَةِ الرُّسُلِ عَلَيْهِ وَعَلَيْهِمُ الصَّلَاةُ وَالسَّلَامُ .
ولا يجاب بأنه جرى على ما نقله وأنَّ قوله : عَلَى الَّذِي قَدْ قَصَرُوا معناه : على الذي قد قصروا سم، عليه، وهو المقصور عليه ؛ لأنَّه يلزم حذف العائد المجرور من غير وجود الشُّروط ؛ إذ مِن جملتها أن يتحد متعلق الحرفين، وهنا لم يتحدا؛ إذ متعلَّق الأوّل: دخول والثَّاني : قصروا فيتعيَّن أنَّ العائد هنا منصوب؛ أي: على الذي قصروه، وهو المقصور.
قوله : (مُسْتَعْمَلٌ) أي : واقع في كلامهم.
قوله : (وَجَيِّدُ) أي : غير شاذ.
He who specifies us with the best of those who were sent, and the best of those who attained the highest stations. And similar to it from: restricted (maqsūr) and restricted upon (maqsūr 'alayhi), and a 'ba' that enters upon one of them permissibly by agreement of both al-Sa'd and al-Sayyid, although the predominant usage according to al-Sa'd is its entry upon the maqsūr, and according to al-Sayyid its entry upon the maqsūr 'alayhi; thus said Ibn Qasim. And Shaykh Yasin refuted him by stating that al-Sayyid explicitly stated what al-Sa'd said in the gloss of al-Mutawwal and elsewhere. By that you know what is in the famous rule (al-dabit), which is the saying of some: 'And the ba' after ikhtisas is disbelieved (yukfiru) its entry upon that which they restricted.' And his saying: 'and similar to it' such as the matter of ikhtisas which is the source of 'ikhtassa', and al-khusus, al-tamyiz, and al-ifrad, unlike the matter of qasr, which is transitive with 'ala. As for the expression 'maqsūr' and 'maqsūr 'alayhi' after the matter of takhsis and its like, it is to clarify the meaning, even if the two matters differ in transitivity. His saying: 'what is in the rule' i.e., that it does not agree with what he transmitted from Sam, nor what he transmitted from the author. And it is answered regarding the rule: that he proceeded in it according to what he transmitted from Yasin; its ultimate is that it contains abbreviation. So his saying: 'the noble scholar al-Sayyid mentioned it' means: and al-Sa'd also, and he only sufficed with al-Sayyid instead of al-Sa'd and did not reverse because the attribution of what he mentioned to al-Sa'd is well-known, unlike its attribution to al-Sayyid. Since it was not well-known, he alerted to it because it is a place of misconception. And its opposite is used and good; the noble imam al-Sayyid mentioned it. When you know that, you know that the apparent meaning from the author's words is its entry upon the maqsūr as is the predominant based on what has passed. So its implication is that he (the Prophet) is restricted to us, not extending to others besides us. But it is not like that because the truth is that he was sent to previous nations; the upshot is that the messengers are his deputies, as the author of al-Burda points to that in his verse: 'For he is a sun of virtue, they are its stars, showing their lights to people in darkness.' And it was answered with several answers; the best of them is that the 'ba' here enters upon the maqsūr 'alayhi, even if it is contrary to the predominant based on what preceded, and the meaning accordingly is that Allah restricted us to him, we do not go beyond him to other messengers. Or that it enters upon the maqsūr as is predominant, but the intended meaning is that Allah specified us with him, may Allah bless him and grant him peace, in terms of his being sent to us directly. That does not contradict that he was also sent to other nations but through the intermediary of the messengers, upon him and them be prayers and peace. And it cannot be answered that he proceeded on what he transmitted and that his saying: 'upon that which they restricted' means upon that which they restricted, i.e., the maqsūr 'alayhi, because that would require dropping the governed genitive pronoun without the conditions being met; among them is that the objects of the two prepositions are the same, but here they are not the same because the object of the first is 'entry' and of the second is 'they restricted'. So it is determined that the pronoun here is in the accusative, i.e., 'upon that which they restricted' (maqsūr). His saying: 'used' means occurring in their speech. His saying: 'and good' means not anomalous.
Source pages p. 87

Commentary
This passage is a multi-layered commentary on the opening line of a poem in logic, specifically discussing the grammatical and logical concept of 'qasr' (restriction) as applied to the Prophet's mission. The core issue is the interpretation of the phrase 'khasşana bi-khayri man ursila' (He specified us with the best of those sent). The commentators analyze the role of the letter 'ba' in this phrase, whether it indicates restriction to the maqsur (the restricted object) or to the maqsur 'alayh (the one to which restriction is made). The discussion begins by noting a disagreement between two scholars, al-Sa'd and al-Sayyid, regarding the predominant usage of the 'ba' after a verb of specification (ikhtisas). Al-Sa'd holds that it typically enters upon the maqsur, while al-Sayyid holds it enters upon the maqsur 'alayh. This is clarified with references to Ibn Qasim and Shaykh Yasin. A famous rule (al-dabit) is cited that says the 'ba' after ikhtisas 'disbelieves' its entry upon the maqsur (i.e., it is not allowed). The commentator then explains that this rule is based on Shaykh Yasin's transmission and that it is an abbreviation: when it says 'al-Sayyid mentioned it', it also includes al-Sa'd, but only al-Sayyid is mentioned because his attribution is less well-known. Next, the passage discusses the apparent implication of the poem's line: that the Prophet is restricted only to his nation. This is problematic because the truth is that he was sent to all previous nations, with the earlier prophets as his deputies. The best answer is that the 'ba' here enters upon the maqsur 'alayh, meaning Allah restricted us to him (we do not go to other messengers). Alternatively, if the 'ba' is upon the maqsur, then the meaning is that He specified us with him in terms of direct sending, but he was still sent to others indirectly. A final grammatical point is made about a proposed interpretation of the rule, showing it is invalid because of mismatch of objects. The passage concludes by clarifying the terms 'musta'mal' (used) and 'jayyid' (good) in the context. Throughout, the commentary provides examples from the Islamic tradition, such as the quote from al-Burda comparing the Prophet to the sun and other prophets to stars.

Key Terms
قصر
المقصور
المقصور عليه
اختصاص
ضابط

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the best answer to resolve the apparent implication that the Prophet is restricted only to his nation?
A) The 'ba' enters on the maqsūr, meaning the Prophet's mission is exclusive to his nation.
B) The 'ba' enters on the maqsūr 'alayh, meaning Allah restricted us to the Prophet and we do not go beyond him to other messengers.
C) The 'ba' is a preposition of cause, not restriction.
D) The rule (ḍābiṭ) is incorrect and should be discarded.

Key Takeaway
The 'ba' in qasr is ambiguous and can indicate restriction on either the subject or the object; the correct interpretation depends on context and must be carefully reasoned to avoid theological or legal error.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Analyze the phrase 'khaṣṣanā Allāhu bi-ramadān' in a hadith. Determine whether the 'ba' likely enters on the maqsūr (Ramadan) or the maqsūr 'alayh (us), and explain the difference in meaning based on the principles from this lesson.
?
Write a brief grammatical argument (like the commentator's) to resolve a disagreement about a verse like 'wa khaṣṣa bihi kull shay'' (and He specified it with everything) -- clarify which element the 'ba' restricts.
