The Term 'Fa'ida' and Its Logical Precision
Commentator defines 'fawa'id' and distinguishes it from three related terms by consideration.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define 'fa'ida' according to the logicians and distinguish it from ghaya, gharad, and 'illa gha'iyya by specifying the aspect (i'tibar) of consideration.

Core Idea
Four terms, one reality: the difference is not in what they refer to, but in how they are regarded.

In this passage, the commentator unpacks the word 'fawa'id' from the matn and provides a precise technical definition. He then clarifies that three other terms — ghaya, gharad, and 'illa gha'iyya — refer to the same underlying interest (maslaha) but are distinguished by the angle from which it is considered. Understanding this distinction is crucial for reading logic texts accurately.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term being defined
Find the word 'fa'ida' in the Arabic text. Note its linguistic definition ('what you gain of knowledge, wealth, or the like') and its terminological definition ('the interest resulting from an action, considered as its fruit and result').
The terminological definition is more precise and limited to actions.
2
Extract the key qualification
The definition adds the phrase 'min haythu innaha thamaratahu wa natijatahu' (from the aspect of being its fruit and result). Understand why this qualification is added: it excludes other ways of looking at the same interest.
This qualification is the differentia that distinguishes fa'ida from the other terms.
3
List the excluded terms and their aspects
Identify the three terms (ghaya, gharad, 'illa gha'iyya) and the specific aspect (i'tibar) that distinguishes each from fa'ida: ghaya considers the interest as at the end of the action, gharad as desired by the agent, 'illa gha'iyya as the motive impelling the agent.
All four terms share the same underlying reality (maslaha); only the consideration changes.
4
Test your understanding
Given a description of an interest, determine which term (fa'ida, ghaya, gharad, or 'illa gha'iyya) is most appropriate based on the aspect mentioned. For example, if the interest is described as 'what the agent seeks', it is gharad.
Practice with examples from fiqh or kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَهَاكَ مِنْ أُصُولِهِ قَواعِدًا تَجْمَعُ مِنْ فُنُونِهِ فَوَائِدَا
الاحتمالاتُ الأَرْبَعَةُ السَّابِقَةُ، وَلِهَذَا قَالَ بَعْضُهُمْ: فِي (مِنْ) وَالإِضَافَةِ هُنَا مَا سَبَقَ فِي قَوْلِهِ: مِنْ أُصُولِهِ، وَهُوَ لَا يَتَمَشَّى إِلَّا عَلَى كَوْنِ المَنْطِقِ يُطْلَقُ عَلَى الفُرُوعِ كَمَا يُطْلَقُ عَلَى الأُصُولِ، فَلْيُتَأَمَّلْ.
قَوْلُهُ: (فَوائِدَا) مَفْعُولٌ بِهِ لِقَوْلِهِ: «تَجْمَعُ».
وَالفَوَائِدُ جَمْعُ: «فَائِدَةِ».
وَهِيَ لُغَةٌ: «مَا اسْتَفَدْتَهُ مِنْ عِلْمٍ، أَوْ مَالٍ، أَوْ نَحْوِهِمَا».
وَاصْطِلاحاً: المَصْلَحَةُ المُتَرَتِّبَةُ عَلَى الفِعْل مِنْ حَيْثُ إِنَّهَا ثَمَرَتُهُ وَنَتِيجَتُهُ.
وَخَرَجَ بِالحَيْثِيَّةِ المَذْكُورَةِ:
- الغَايَةُ، فَإِنَّهَا تِلْكَ المَصْلَحَةُ مِنْ حَيْثُ إِنَّهَا فِي طَرَفِ الفِعْلِ.
- وَالغَرَضُ، فَإِنَّهُ المَصْلَحَةُ المَذْكُورَةُ مِنْ حَيْثُ إِنَّهَا مَطْلُوبَةٌ لِلفَاعِلِ مِنَ الفِعْلِ.
- وَالعِلَّةُ الغَائِيَّةُ، فَإِنَّهَا تِلْكَ المَصْلَحَةُ مِنْ حَيْثُ إِنَّهَا بَاعِثَةٌ لِلفَاعِلِ عَلَى الفِعْلِ.
Here is from its principles rules that gather from its arts benefits. The four preceding probabilities, and because of this some said: In 'min' and the idafa here, what preceded in his saying 'from its principles', and it only proceeds on the understanding that mantiq is applied to the branches as it is applied to the principles, so let it be considered. His saying: 'benefits' is an object complement for his saying 'gather'. And 'benefits' is plural of 'fa'ida'. It is linguistically: 'what you have gained of knowledge, or wealth, or the like'. And terminologically: 'the interest resulting from the action in terms of it being its fruit and result'. And by the mentioned aspect, the following are excluded: - The goal (ghaya), for it is that same interest in terms of it being at the end of the action. - The purpose (gharad), for it is that mentioned interest in terms of it being desired by the agent from the action. - The final cause ('illa gha'iyya), for it is that same interest in terms of it being an impetus for the agent upon the action.
Source pages p. 127

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the matn line 'Here is from its principles rules that gather from its arts benefits.' The commentator begins by pointing out the four possible interpretations of the particle 'min' and the possessive construction in 'from its principles'. He notes that some have said that the same options apply here as in an earlier phrase, and that this only makes sense if the term 'mantiq' is applied to both the principles and the branches. Then he focuses on the word 'fawa'id' (benefits), explaining it is the object of 'gather'. He gives a linguistic definition: whatever one gains of knowledge, wealth, or the like. The terminological definition is 'the interest (maslaha) resulting from an action, considered as its fruit and result.' By adding the qualification 'from the aspect of being a fruit and result', he excludes three related terms: ghaya (the same interest but considered as being at the end of the action), gharad (the interest considered as desired by the agent), and 'illa gha'iyya (the interest considered as what motivates the agent). This shows how different i'tibarat (considerations) yield distinct technical terms while the underlying reality is the same.

Key Terms

Key Takeaway
The four terms fa'ida, ghaya, gharad, and 'illa gha'iyya refer to the same underlying interest but are distinguished by the logical consideration (i'tibar) applied: as fruit, as end, as aim, or as motive.

Distinguishing the Four Terms: Fa'ida, Ghaya, Gharad, and 'Illa Gha'iyya
Clarifying the relationship between fa'ida, ghaya, gharad, and 'illa gha'iyya.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between fa'ida, ghaya, gharad, and 'illa gha'iyya and apply them to scenarios in logic and Islamic jurisprudence.

Core Idea
Four terms, one essence: each considers the same interest from a different angle.

In this passage, the commentator clarifies the relationship between four key terms in logic: fa'ida (benefit/interest), ghaya (end), gharad (desired outcome), and 'illa gha'iyya (final cause). Though they are identical in essence, each term highlights a different aspect of the same reality. By working through the example of digging for water and finding a treasure, you will learn how to distinguish these terms and understand their relative generality. This precision is crucial for analyzing purposes and causes in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four terms and their shared essence
Notice that the passage states the four terms are 'identical in essence' (muttaḥida bi al-dhāt) but 'differ by consideration' (mukhtalifa bi al-iʿtibār). This means they refer to the same underlying reality, but each highlights a distinct aspect.
The terms are not separate entities; they are conceptual distinctions.
2
Extract the specific consideration of each term
From the context, fa'ida is the benefit considered as a result, ghaya is the end point, gharad is the desired outcome, and 'illa gha'iyya is the motive that impels the agent. These considerations determine when each term applies.
The key is the perspective (iʿtibār): result, end, desire, motive.
3
Apply the terms to the digging example
In the example: someone digs intending to find water (gharad and 'illa gha'iyya would apply to the water). A treasure appears at the end. The treasure is a fa'ida (benefit) and a ghaya (end), but not a gharad nor 'illa gha'iyya because it was neither desired nor the motive.
This shows that fa'ida and ghaya are more general than the other two.
4
Analyze the refutation about fa'ida being more general than ghaya
Some claim that if the treasure appears halfway and digging continues, the treasure is only a fa'ida, not a ghaya. The refutation argues that the action that produced the treasure ends at its appearance, making it a ghaya of that sub-action. The rest is a new action.
This refines the understanding: ghaya applies to the end of the action that generates the benefit.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
سَمَّيْتُهُ بِـ: «السُّلَّمِ المُرَوْنَقِ يُرْقَى بِهِ سَمَاءُ عِلْمِ المَنْطِقِ
فَالأَرْبَعَةُ مُتَّحِدَةٌ بِالذَّاتِ مُخْتَلِفَةٌ بِالاعْتِبَارِ، لَكِنَّ الأَوْلَانِ أَعَمُّ مِنَ الأَخِيرَيْنِ مُطْلَقاً؛ لانْفِرَادِهِمَا عَنْهُمَا فِي مَا لَوْ حَفَرَ مُرِيدُ المَاءِ، فَظَهَرَ لَهُ كَنْزٌ، فَإِنَّهُ يُقَالُ لَهُ: «فَائِدَةٌ»، وَ: «غَايَةٌ»، لَا غَرَضَ وَ: «عِلَّةٌ غَائِيَّةٌ»؛ لِأَنَّهُ لَيْسَ مَطْلُوباً مِنَ الفِعْلِ، وَلَا بَاعِثًا عَلَيْهِ.
قَالَ بَعْضُهُمْ: الفَائِدَةُ أَيْضاً أَعَمُّ مُطْلَقاً مِنَ الغَايَةِ؛ لِانْفِرَادِهَا عَنْهَا فِي مَا لَوْ حَفَرَ مُرِيدُ المَاءِ، فَظَهَرَ لَهُ كَنْزٌ عَلَى نِصْفِ الحَفْرِ مَثَلاً، وَلَمْ يَقْطَع الحَفْرَ بَلْ أَتَمَّهُ فَإِنَّهُ يُقَالُ لِهَذَا الكَنْزِ: «فَائِدَةُ» وَلَا: «غَايَةٌ»؛ لِأَنَّهُ لَيْسَ فِي طَرَفِ الفِعْلِ.
وَرُدَّ: بِأَنَّهُ فِي طَرَفِ الفِعْلِ الَّذِي انْتَهَى بِوُجُودِ الكَنْزِ، وَأَمَّا مَا بَعْدَهُ فَفِعْلٌ جَدِيدٌ، فَتَأَمَّلْ.
قَوْلُهُ: (سَمَّيْتُهُ) الضَّمِيرُ يَرْجِعُ لِلمُؤَلّفِ المَفْهُومِ مِنَ السِّيَاقِ، فَالمُسَمَّى إِنَّمَا هُوَ الأَلْفَاظُ المُؤَلَّفَةُ، لَكِنْ بِاعْتِبَارِ دَلَالَتِهَا عَلَى المَعَانِي، كَمَا هُوَ التَّحْقِيقُ مِنَ احْتِمَالَاتٍ سَبْعَةٍ أَبْدَاهَا السَّيِّدُ الجُرْجَانِيُّ.
I named it 'al-Sullam al-Murawnaq' by which the sky of the science of logic is ascended. So the four are identical in essence, differing by consideration; but the first two are absolutely more general than the last two, because they are uniquely found without them in the case of one who digs intending water, and a treasure appears to him. For it is called 'fa'ida' and 'ghaya', not 'gharad' nor 'illa gha'iyya', because it is not desired from the action nor an impetus for it. Some said: fa'ida is also absolutely more general than ghaya, because it is uniquely found without ghaya in the case of one who digs intending water, and a treasure appears to him halfway through digging, and he does not stop digging but completes it. For this treasure is called 'fa'ida' and not 'ghaya', because it is not at the end of the action. And it was refuted: that it is at the end of the action that ended with the existence of the treasure, and what follows it is a new action, so consider. His saying: 'I named it' – the pronoun refers to the author understood from context; the named thing is only the composed words, but by consideration of their indication of meanings, as is the verification from among the seven possibilities that al-Sayyid al-Jurjani propounded.
Source pages p. 128

Commentary
This passage continues the commentary on the four terms. It begins by citing the matn line that names the work 'al-Sullam al-Murawnaq'. Then it returns to the relationship between fa'ida, ghaya, gharad, and 'illa gha'iyya. The commentator states they are identical in essence (dhat) but differ by consideration (i'tibar). However, he notes that the first two (fa'ida and ghaya) are absolutely more general than the last two (gharad and 'illa gha'iyya), because there are cases where fa'ida and ghaya apply but not the others. He gives the example of someone digging for water and finding a treasure; the treasure is a fa'ida and a ghaya, but not a gharad (since it was not desired) nor an 'illa gha'iyya (since it was not the motive). Then he presents a further claim by some that fa'ida is even more general than ghaya, using the scenario of a treasure appearing halfway and the digging continues; the treasure is a fa'ida but not a ghaya because it is not at the end. He records a refutation: the treasure is at the end of the action that ended with its appearance, and whatever follows is a new action. Thus the claim is corrected. Finally, he explains the naming of the work: the pronoun in 'I named it' refers to the author, and the named entity is the composed words, but considered as indicating meanings. This is one of seven possibilities that al-Sayyid al-Jurjani proposed. This passage demonstrates how terminological precision is achieved through hypothetical cases and objections.

غاية
غرض
علة غائية

Key Takeaway
Fa'ida and ghaya are broader in scope than gharad and 'illa gha'iyya because they do not require intention or motivation.

Distinguishing Benefit and Goal from Purpose and Final Cause
Bajuri distinguishes the four terms and explains their generality relationships.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between benefit/goal and purpose/final cause using the criteria of being intended and being terminal.

Core Idea
Benefit and goal are broader because they can occur without being intended.

In Islamic logic, terms like 'benefit' and 'goal' are often used interchangeably, but Bajuri shows they have distinct scopes. This lesson will help you parse the nuanced relationship between these four concepts, which is essential for analyzing causal explanations in Islamic theology and jurisprudence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four terms
From the Arabic passage, list the four terms: فَائِدَة (benefit), غَايَة (goal), غَرَض (purpose), عِلَّة غَائِيَّة (final cause).
They are introduced as united in essence but different in consideration.
2
Understand the generality claim
Bajuri states that the first two are absolutely more general than the last two. This means that any instance of purpose or final cause is also an instance of benefit and goal, but not vice versa.
The generality is in terms of essence and locus, not conceptual definition.
3
Apply the digging example
Consider someone digging for water. If water appears at the end, it is both a benefit (useful outcome) and a goal (the endpoint). But it is not called a purpose or final cause because it was not the intended object motivating the action.
The water is not desired from the action nor does it motivate the action, so it lacks the intentionality required for purpose and final cause.
4
Check the Shershimi commentary
Shershimi clarifies that the generality is in the essence and the locus (where they occur), not in the concept. Conceptually, all four are distinct because each has a different aspect or consideration.
This guards against conflating the terms in technical discussions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَالأَرْبَعَةُ مُتَّحِدَةٌ بِالذَّاتِ مُخْتَلِفَةٌ بِالاعْتِبَارِ، لَكِنَّ الأَوْلَانِ أَعَمُّ مِنَ الأَخِيرَيْنِ (۱) مُطْلَقاً؛ لانْفِرَادِهِمَا عَنْهُمَا فِي مَا لَوْ حَفَرَ مُرِيدُ المَاءِ، فَظَهَرَ لَهُ كَيْرٌ (٢) ، فَإِنَّهُ يُقَالُ لَهُ : «فَائِدَةٌ» ، وَ: «غَايَةٌ»، لَا : غَرَضِ | و: «عِلَّةٌ غَائِيَّةٌ»؛ لِأَنَّهُ لَيْسَ مَطْلُوباً مِنَ الفِعْلِ، وَلَا بَاعِنَا عَلَيْهِ. قَالَ بَعْضُهُمْ : الفَائِدَةُ أَيْضاً أَعَمُّ مُطلَقاً مِنَ الغَايَةِ؛ لِانْفِرَادِهَا عَنْهَا فِي مَا لَوْ حَفَرَ مُرِيدُ المَاءِ، فَظَهَرَ لَهُ كَنْزٌ عَلَى نِصْفِ الحَفْرِ مَثَلاً ، وَلَمْ يَقْطَع الحَفْرَ بَلْ أَتَمَّهُ فَإِنَّهُ يُقَالُ لِهَذَا الكَنْزِ: «فَائِدَةُ» لَا : « غَايَةٌ ؛ لِأَنَّهُ لَيْسَ فِي طَرَفِ الفِعْلِ. وَرُدَّ : بِأَنَّهُ فِي طَرَفِ الفِعْلِ الَّذِي انْتَهَى بِوُجُودِ الكَنْزِ، وَأَمَّا مَا بَعْدَهُ فَفِعْلٌ جَدِيدٌ ، فَتَأَمَّلْ . (۱) الشرشيمي : قوله : (أَعَمُّ مِنَ الأَخِيرَيْنِ) أي: أعمُّ مِن حيث الذات والمحل الذي يتحققان فيه، وأما باعتبار المفهوم فالأربعة متباينة؛ لاختلاف كل بحيثية واعتبار ؛ تأمل . (٢) الشرشيمي : قوله : (فَظَهَرَ لَهُ كَنْزُ أي : في آخر الحفر .
The four are united in essence but different in consideration. However, the first two are absolutely more general than the last two, because they are exclusive to them in the case where someone seeking water digs and a treasure appears for him. So it is said of it: 'benefit' and 'goal', not 'purpose' and 'final cause', because it is not desired from the action nor does it motivate it. Some said: Benefit is also absolutely more general than goal, because it is exclusive to it in the case where someone seeking water digs, and a treasure appears to him at half the digging, for example, and he did not stop digging but completed it. So this treasure is called 'benefit' not 'goal', because it is not at the endpoint of the action. And it was rebutted: that it is at the endpoint of the action which ended with the existence of the treasure. As for what comes after, it is a new action. So reflect. (1) Shershimi: His statement: 'more general than the last two' means more general in terms of essence and the locus in which they occur. But in terms of concept, the four are distinct; each differs by aspect and consideration. Reflect. (2) Shershimi: His statement: 'a treasure appeared to him' means at the end of the digging.
Source pages p. 128

Commentary
This passage is a definitional and comparative analysis of four terms related to the ends of actions: فائدة (benefit), غاية (goal), غرض (purpose), and علة غائية (final cause). The author (Bajuri) explains that these four are identical in their underlying reality but differ in how they are considered. The key claim is that the first two (benefit and goal) are unconditionally more general than the last two (purpose and final cause). This is because benefit and goal can exist even when the action is not intended for them, unlike purpose and final cause which are directly intended. The example given is of someone digging for water: if water appears, it is both a benefit and a goal; but it is not called a purpose or final cause because it was not the intended object of the action nor the motive. A counterclaim is then presented: benefit is even more general than goal. The example is of a treasure discovered midway through digging; the digger does not stop but completes the digging. The treasure is a benefit but not the goal because it is not at the endpoint of the act. The rebuttal argues that the treasure is indeed at the endpoint of that portion of the act that concluded with its discovery; what follows is a new act. Thus the original distinction stands. The Shershimi marginal note clarifies that the generality is in terms of the essence and locus, not the concept, where the four are distinct. This passage guards against conflating these terms, especially in causal analysis in Islamic sciences (e.g., distinguishing the benefit of a ritual from its intended goal).

Key Terms
فائدة
غاية
غرض
علة غائية

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) Because it is a treasure, not water

Key Takeaway
Benefit and goal are broader terms that can include unintended outcomes, while purpose and final cause always involve intention and motivation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the act of performing wudu: the benefit is purification and physical cleanliness; the goal is the completion of the washing; the purpose is to fulfill the command for prayer; the final cause is to achieve spiritual readiness. Benefit vs Goal: The Halfway Treasure Example
Bajuri presents a counterclaim about benefit being more general than goal and its rebuttal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate the claim that benefit is more general than goal and assess the rebuttal that the treasure is at the endpoint of a sub-action.

Core Idea
Benefit can appear before the goal; the rebuttal shows that any benefit is at the endpoint of some action segment.

Some scholars argued that benefit is even more general than goal, citing an example of a treasure discovered halfway through digging. This lesson examines that counterclaim and its rebuttal, revealing a fine point about how we define the 'action' and its endpoint. This nuance is crucial when analyzing causal sequences in Islamic legal reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the counterclaim
The passage says 'some said: Benefit is also absolutely more general than goal'. This is a further refinement.
2
Examine the halfway treasure example
Someone digs for water but finds a treasure at half the depth. He does not stop but continues. The treasure is a benefit because it is useful, but it is not the goal (ghaya) because the goal is the water at the full depth.
The treasure is not at the endpoint of the entire digging action.
3
Understand the rebuttal
The rebuttal argues that the treasure is at the endpoint of the sub-action that ended with its discovery. The continuation is a new action. Therefore the treasure is at the endpoint of that segment, making it both a benefit and a goal (of that segment).
This preserves the original claim that benefit and goal coincide at endpoints.
4
Reflect on the Shershimi confirmation
Shershimi's note confirms that the treasure appears 'at the end of the digging' (i.e., of the initial segment), supporting the rebuttal.

Source Text

Book Titles as Transferred Proper Nouns
Bajuri explains the title and discusses whether book names are proper nouns or common nouns.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between literal, metaphorical, and transferred proper noun meanings of a book title, and classify book names as ʻalam shakhṣ or ʻalam jins.

Core Idea
A book title is a transferred proper noun — literal in its naming, even if originally metaphorical.

When a book is named, the title may carry layers of meaning. In this passage, al-Bajuri explains how the title 'al-Sullam' functions: though 'ladder' is a metaphor for the book's purpose, as a proper noun it becomes literal. He also clarifies whether book names are proper nouns for individuals or generic proper nouns, with implications for how we understand naming in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the debated entity
Locate the discussion of what 'the named' (المسمّى) refers to in a book title. Al-Jurjani lists seven possibilities: expressions only, meanings only, mental forms, or combinations. The preferred view is expressions alone, but with observation of meanings.
This sets up the conceptual framework for understanding what a book title denotes.
2
Analyze the word 'ladder'
Examine how 'سلم' is used. In physical sense it is literal; applied to a book it is an explicit metaphor. However, as the proper name of this specific book, it becomes a transferred proper noun (علم منقول), shifting to literal status in the naming context.
Distinguish between the word's general metaphorical use and its specific proper noun use.
3
Classify the type of proper noun
Determine whether book names are ʻalam shakhṣ (proper noun of an individual) or ʻalam jins (generic proper noun). The passage concludes they are ʻalam shakhṣ because each book name refers to a unique set of expressions and meanings, not multiplied by copies.
Contrast with names of sciences (e.g., 'النحو', 'المنطق') which are ʻalam jins according to the well-known view.
4
Apply the classification to other book titles
Check if other book titles (e.g., 'Al-Kafi', 'Al-Muqaddima') fit the ʻalam shakhṣ or ʻalam jins category based on the criteria given. Note that chapter names (تراجم) are also ʻalam shakhṣ.
This step reinforces the rule by applying it to familiar examples.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قَوْلُهُ : (سَمَّيْتُهُ) الضَّمِيرُ يَرْجِعُ لِلمُؤَلّفِ المَفْهُومِ مِنَ السِّيَاقِ، فَالمُسَمَّى إِنَّمَا هُوَ الأَلْفَاظُ المُؤَلَّفَةُ، لَكِنْ بِاعْتِبَارِ (۳) دَلَالَتِهَا عَلَى المَعَانِي، كَمَا هُوَ التَّحْقِيقُ مِنَ احْتِمَالَاتٍ سَبْعَةٍ أَبْدَاهَا السَّيِّدُ الجُرْجَانِيُّ فِي مُسَمَّى الكُتُبِ حَيْثُ قَالَ : يُحْتَمَلُ أَنَّهُ الأَلْفَاظُ فَقَط ، أوِ المَعَانِي فَقَطْ ، أَوِ النُّقُوسُ فَقَدْ ، أَوِ اثْنَانِ مِنْ هَذِهِ الثَّلَاثَةِ، أَوْ مَجْمُوعُ الثَّلَاثَةِ . والمُخْتَارُ الأَوَّلُ ، لَكِنْ بِقَيْدِ مُلَاحَظَةِ المَعَانِي كَمَا عَلِمْتَ . قَوْلُهُ : (بِالسُّلَّم) مَفْعُولٌ ثَانٍ لِـ «(سَمَّى»، وَأَدْخَلَ البَاءَ» عَلَيْهِ؛ لِأَنَّهُ يَجُوزُ تَعَدِّيهِ إِلَيْهِ بِهَا ، كَمَا يَجُوزُ تَعَدِّيهِ إِلَيْهِ بِنَفْسِهِ؛ تَقُولُ : سَمَّيْتُ ابْنِي بِمُحَمَّدٍ، وَسَمَّيْتُهُ مُحَمَّد )) . وَالسُّلَّمُ حَقِيقَةٌ فِيمَا يُتَوَصَّلُ بِهِ إِلَى أَعْلَى، إِذَا كَانَ ذَلِكَ الأَمْرُ مَحْسُوساً بِحَاسَةِ البَصَرِ، وَإِلَّا كَانَ مجازاً بِالاسْتِعَارَةِ التَّصْرِيحِيَّةِ كَمَا هُنَا ، لَكِنْ جَعَلَهُ هُنَا (۲) مجازاً بِقَطْعِ النَّظَرِ عَنِ العَلَمِيَّةِ، وَإِلَّا فَهُوَ حَقِيقَةٌ لِوَضْعِهِ عَلَى هَذَا المَتْنِ بِطَرِيقِ النَّقْلِ ، لَا لِمَا قِيلَ (۳) مِنْ أَنَّهُ صَارَ حَقِيقَةٌ عُرْفِيَّةٌ فِيهِ، فَهُوَ مِنَ الأَعْلَامِ المَنْقُولَةِ، وَهِيَ حَقَائِقُ . وَاعْلَمْ أَنَّ أَسْمَاءَ الكُتُبِ - وَمِثْلَهَا أَسْمَاءُ التَّرَاجِمِ - مِنْ قَبِيلِ عَلَمِ الشَّخْصِ؛ لِأَنَّ المُسَمَّى بِهَا - الَّذِي هُوَ الأَلْفَاظُ المَخْصُوصَةُ الدَّالَّة عَلَى المَعَانِي المَخْصُوصَةِ - مُشَخَصٌ مُعَيَّنٌ، وَلَا نَظَرَ لِتَعَدُّدِهِ بِتَعَدُدِ المَحَلِّ ؛ لِأَنَّهُ إِنَّمَا يَنْشَأ عَنِ التَّدْقِيقِ الفَلْسَفِيّ ، وَهُوَ غَيْرُ مُعْتَبَر عِنْدَ أَرْبَابِ العَرَبِيَّةِ كَمَا حَقَّقَهُ العصامُ فِي شَرْحِ رِسَالَةِ الوَضْعِ»، بِخِلَافِ أَسْمَاءِ العُلُومِ، فَإِنَّهَا مِنْ قَبِيلِ عَلَمِ الجِنْسِ عَلَى المَشْهُورِ،
His statement: 'I named it' — the pronoun refers to the author understood from context. The named is the composed expressions, but considering their indication of meanings, as is the verification from seven possibilities that al-Sayyid al-Jurjani presented concerning the named of books, where he said: It is possible that it is only the expressions, or only the meanings, or only the mental forms, or two of these three, or the totality of the three. And the chosen is the first, but with the condition of observing the meanings, as you have learned. His statement: 'with the ladder' is the second object of 'named', and he inserted the preposition 'with' because it is permissible for it to be transitive with it, just as it is permissible for it to be transitive without it; you say: 'I named my son Muhammad' or 'I named my son with Muhammad'. And 'ladder' is literal in what is used to ascend to a higher place when that thing is perceptible by sight; otherwise it is metaphorical by explicit metaphor, as here. However, he considered it here as metaphorical disregarding the status of being a proper noun; otherwise it is literal due to its being placed on this text by way of transference, not because it became a conventional reality in it. So it is among transferred proper nouns, and these are literals. And know that names of books — and similarly names of chapters — are of the type of proper noun of an individual (ʻalam shakhṣ), because what is named by them — which is the specific expressions denoting specific meanings — is a specified individual, and no consideration is given to its multiplicity by multiplicity of location, because that arises from philosophical precision, which is not considered among Arabic scholars, as al-ʻIṣām verified in his commentary on the Epistle on Usage (Risālat al-Waḍʻ). Unlike names of sciences, for they are of the type of generic proper noun (ʻalam jins) according to the well-known view.
Source pages p. 128

Commentary
This passage is a definitional and analytical commentary on the opening line of al-Sullam al-Murawnaq, focusing on the title itself. It begins by explaining the pronoun in 'سميته' (I named it) and notes that the object named is the composed expressions, but with consideration of their meanings. This relates to a known discussion by al-Sayyid al-Jurjani on seven possibilities for what a book's name signifies: expressions only, meanings only, mental forms, combinations, or all three. The preferred view is that it is the expressions alone, but with the condition that the meanings are taken into account. Then the author discusses the word 'سلم' (ladder). In its literal sense, it refers to a physical ladder used for climbing; when applied to a book, it is a clear metaphor (استعارة تصريحية). However, because 'al-Sullam' is the proper name of this book, it is a transferred proper noun (علم منقول), and thus it is considered literal in that context, not metaphorical. The author emphasizes that this is due to the transference of the word from its original meaning to become a name, not because it gained a conventional meaning. Finally, a general rule is given: names of books and chapters are proper nouns for individuals (علم شخص), since they refer to a specific set of expressions with specific meanings. Multiplicity of copies does not affect this, as such considerations are philosophical and not standard among Arabic grammarians. In contrast, names of sciences (like 'النحو' or 'المنطق') are considered generic proper nouns (علم جنس). The Anbabi commentary clarifies the chosen view and the condition of meaning. This lesson is crucial for understanding how technical terms in logic are used and how naming conventions operate in the Islamic scholarly tradition.

Key Terms
علم الشخص
علم الجنس
الاستعارة التصريحية
الأعلام المنقولة

Key Takeaway
Book titles are proper nouns for individuals (ʻalam shakhṣ), and a title like 'Al-Sullam' is literal due to being a transferred proper noun, not metaphorical in the naming context.

Are Names of Sciences Proper or Generic? A Logical Distinction
Author discusses whether names of sciences are proper names or generic names.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the two scholarly views on the classification of science names and apply al-Jurjani's criteria to determine whether a given science name is a proper name or a generic name.

Core Idea
The classification of a science name hinges on whether the intended referent is the rules themselves or individual perceptions of those rules.

The names of Islamic sciences such as 'Fiqh' and 'Mantiq' are commonly used, but their logical status is debated. Are they proper names designating unique entities, or generic names indicating universals? This lesson examines the arguments from leading scholars, including al-Jurjani's critical distinction between the fixed rules (qawa‘id) and the perceptions (idrakat) of those rules. Understanding this distinction sharpens your grasp of how definitions work in the Islamic tradition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two opposing views
Find in the text the claim that science names are proper names and the claim that they are generic names. Note the reasoning for each.
The proper name view: the named entity is the specific rulings, a specified individual. The generic name view: the name refers to a universal quiddity that increases with scholars.
2
Analyze the reasoning for the proper name view
The text says the named entity (the specific rulings) is a specified individual, and multiplicity of place is not considered. What does this imply about rules?
The rules (qawa‘id) are considered a single unit, not multiplied by the scholars who hold them.
3
Analyze al-Jurjani's distinction
According to the passage, when the name refers to the rules (qawa‘id), it is a proper name because the rules are identical across minds. When it refers to perceptions (idrakat), it is a generic name because perceptions differ. Understand why.
Perceptions are personal and multiply with individuals, while rules are universal and shared.
4
Apply the distinction
Given a science name like 'Usul al-Fiqh', decide whether it functions as a proper or generic name depending on the context. Justify using al-Jurjani's criteria.
If referring to the set of principles (rules), it is proper; if referring to each scholar's understanding, it is generic.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لَكِنِ اخْتَارَ بَعْضُ المُحَقِّقِينَ أَنَّهَا مِنْ قَبِيلِ عَلَمِ الشَّخْصِ أَيْضاً ، لأَنَّ المُسَمَّى بِهَا الَّذِي هُوَ الأَحْكَامُ المَخصُوصَةُ مُشَخَصٌ مُعَيَّنٌ، وَلَا نَظَرَ لِتَعَدُّدِهِ بِتَعَدُّدِ المَحَلِّ كَمَا ذُكِرَ ، فَلْيُفهم . الأنبابي : كخيطين ضعيفين ضُمَّا من غير فتل، فضعفهما باقٍ، بخلافه على الثَّاني، فإنَّه مثل الخيطين بعد الفتل، فيزول الضَّعف؛ لأنَّهما صارا شيئاً واحداً، وأنت خبير بأنَّ الذي اختاره المحقق الدُّوَّانيُّ، وعليه مشايخنا منهم شيخنا المحشّي - نفعنا الله تعالى ببركته - ؛ إذ كان يقرر لنا ذلك كثيراً : إنَّما هو الألفاظ الذهنيَّة وهي ليست مِنَ السَّبعة؛ بدليل تعليلهم عدم صحة مدلوليتها، بأنَّها أعراض تنقضي بمجرَّد النُّطق، فلا تصلح أن تكون مدلولاً، ولا جزء مدلول، ويستفاد أيضاً من ظاهر كلام السَّيِّد الجرجاني في حاشيته على المطوّل»، فقول المحشّي : (والمختار الأوّل فيه نظر، ولولا هذا لجعلت) (مِنْ) في قوله: «مِنِ احْتِمَالَاتِ سَبْعَة بمعنى: باء» البدل . إلا أن يقال: أرادوا بالألفاظ ما يشمل الألفاظ الذهنيَّة غاية الأمر: أنَّ في التعليل قصوراً، فقولهم : لأنَّها أعراض سيَّالة ... إلخ؛ أي: ولأنَّها غير مقصودة لذاتها، فالأوّل راجع للألفاظ الخارجيَّة | والثاني لها وللذهنيّة. لكن إذا نظرت للألفاظ الذّهنيَّة زيادة على الألفاظ الخارجيَّة، ولكون المعاني وما معها شروطاً أو شطوراً، زادت الاحتمالات على سبعة، وقد بينا ذلك فيما كتبناه على «القطر» وحاشيته للعلامة الشجاعي(۳) قوله : (لَكِنِ اخْتَارَ بَعْضُ المُحَقِّقِينَ ... إلخ) [ب/۱۸] أي: لأنَّ مدلولها القواعد المضبوطة الحاصلة بالفعل أو بالقوَّة، فالواضع استحضرها بجهة وحدتها ووضع عليها الاسم المخصوص. قوله : (وَلَا نَظَرَ لِتَعَدُّدِهِ بِتَعَدُّدِ المَحَلِّ قد يقال : القول بأنَّ أسماء العلوم مِن قبيل أعلام الأجناس ليس مبنيا على أنَّ الشَّيء يتعدد بتعدد محله الذي هو تدقيق فلسفي، بل لأنَّه لما كانت أسماء العلوم أسماء قابلة للزيادة؛ لأنَّها تزيد بزيادة العلماء كانت أسماء لماهيَّات القواعد الكلية الشاملة، تلك الماهيات لما حصل بالفعل ولما لم يحصل منها كـ «أسامة») اسمٌ للماهيَّة الشاملة لما حصل مِنَ الإفراد بالفعل ولما لم يحصل، بخلاف أسماء الكتب والتراجم، فإنَّها أسماء لأشياء لا تقبل الزيادة؛ إذ هي للقواعد، وهي لما حصل بالفعل. (1) في بعض النسخ وكذلك المطبوع: «لما» بدلاً من كما ». (۲) في (ج) : «لجعلنا» بدلاً من «لجعلت». (۳) انظر : تقرير الأنبابي على حاشية السجاعي على القطر» (ص: ۱۹) الطبعة الحجرية . أسماء I  ۱۳۱ مقدمة الناظم . (later part) ... الأنبابي • هذا ؛ ونقل سم عنِ أنَّ السيد الجرجاني : بعضهم يفصل في أسماء العلوم بين أن يراد بها القواعد أو الإدراكات، فجعلها على الأوّل أعلاماً شخصيَّةً؛ معللاً بأنَّ القواعد التي في ذهن زيد هي التي في ذهن عمرو من غير نظر إلى تعدد المحلّ، وعلى الثَّاني أعلاماً جنسيَّةً ؛ معللاً بأنَّ الإدراك أمر كلّي له أفراد متميّزة بالشَّخص ؛ ضرورةً أنَّ إدراك زيدٍ يغاير إدراك عمرو، وإن لم ينظر إلى المحل بخلاف ما سبق، فإنَّ التَّمايز فيه إنَّما جاء مِن محله . اهـ . وسكت عمَّا إذا أريد بها المَلَكَة والظاهرُ أنَّها كالإدراك (٦) ، والظاهر أنَّ هذا التفصيل جارٍ على القول (۱) الشرشيمي : قوله : (كَمَا اشْتُهِرَ) أي : على ألسنة الناس. (۲) الشرشيمي : قوله : لَكِنَّ المَرْوِيَّ عَنِ المُصَنِّفِ) أي : المنقول عنه . (۳) الشرشيمي : قوله : بَعْدَ أَنْ رَأى الخَطَّيْنِ ) أي : بعد أن رأى الشاعر ... إلخ. (٤) الشرشيمي : قوله : (وَهَذَا عَلَيْهِ رَوْنَقُ الخَط ... إلخ) أفاد أنَّ خطّ الملك أعظم من وجهين . (٥) الشرشيمي : قوله : (وَرُدَّ ذَلِكَ الاسْتِشْهَادُ أي : فلم يثبت بهذا ثبوته [۱۷/۱] عن العرب، فلا بدَّ مِن استشهادٍ آخر . (٦) في (ب) : «كا لإدراكات» بدلاً من كالإدراك».
But some of the investigators chose that they (names of sciences) are from the category of proper names too, because the named entity which is the specific rulings is a specified individual, and no consideration is given to its multiplicity by multiplicity of place as was mentioned, so understand. Al-Anbabi: Like two weak threads joined without twisting, so their weakness remains; contrary to the second, for it is like two threads after twisting, their weakness disappears because they become one thing. And you are aware that what al-Muhaqqiq al-Dawwani chose, and upon it our teachers, among them our teacher the commenter (may Allah benefit us through his blessing), used to frequently affirm to us, is only mental utterances, and they are not from the seven; because of their reasoning for the invalidity of their signification: that they are accidents that pass away with mere utterance, so they are not suitable to be signified nor part of signified. And it is also understood from the apparent meaning of al-Sayyid al-Jurjani's words in his commentary on al-Mutawwal. So the commenter's statement: 'And the first choice is questionable, and were it not for this I would have made 'min' in his statement 'from the seven probabilities' with the meaning of ba' al-badal.' Except it could be said: They intended by 'utterances' that which includes mental utterances, the only issue is that there is a deficiency in the reasoning. So their statement 'because they are fleeting accidents...' means: and because they are not intended for themselves. So the first refers to external utterances, and the second to them and to mental utterances. But if you consider mental utterances in addition to external utterances, and because meanings and what accompanies them are conditions or parts, the probabilities increase beyond seven, as we have explained in what we wrote on al-Qatr and its commentary by al-Allama al-Shuja'i. His statement: (But some of the investigators chose... etc.) [b/18] meaning: because their signified are the fixed rules actually or potentially attained. So the coiner conceived them in respect of their unity and placed upon them the specific name. His statement: (And no consideration to its multiplicity by multiplicity of place...) It could be said: The statement that names of sciences are from the category of generic names is not based on the fact that a thing multiplies by multiplicity of its place, which is a philosophical precision. Rather, because when names of sciences are names that admit increase because they increase with the increase of scholars, they are names for the quiddities of universal rules that encompass those quiddities, whether actually attained or not, like 'Usama' (the lion) is a name for the universal quiddity that encompasses what has actually occurred of individuals and what has not. Contrary to names of books and biographies, for they are names for things that do not admit increase, because they are for rules that have actually occurred. (Footnote 1: In some copies, 'lama' instead of 'kama'. Footnote 2: In (c), 'laja'alna' instead of 'laja'altu'. Footnote 3: See: Taqrir al-Anbabi 'ala Hashiyat al-Sujai'i 'ala al-Qatr, p. 19, lithograph edition.) Names I [PAGE_131] 131 Introduction of the author. (12) I named it 'al-Sullam al-Murawnaq' by which one ascends to the heavens of the science of logic. al-Bajuri (1) His statement: (al-Murawnaq)... (later part) ... al-Anbabi: This; and it is transmitted from al-Sayyid al-Jurjani: Some differentiate regarding names of sciences between intending by them the rules or the perceptions. They make them on the first [view] proper names, reasoning that the rules in the mind of Zaid are the same as those in the mind of 'Amr without consideration of multiplicity of place. And on the second [view] generic names, reasoning that perception is a universal with individuals distinguished by person; necessarily, Zaid's perception differs from 'Amr's perception even if one does not look at the place, unlike what preceded, for the distinction there came only from its place. End. And he remained silent about when the disposition (al-malaka) is intended, and the apparent is that it is like perception (6) [In (b): 'ka al-idrakat' instead of 'ka al-idrak']. And the apparent is that this differentiation is valid on the view... (Marginal notes from al-Sharshami).
Source pages p. 130

Commentary
This passage is a detailed discussion within the hashiya about the nature of names of sciences such as 'Fiqh' or 'Mantiq'. The author first notes that some investigators consider these names to be proper names (علم الشخص) because they refer to specific sets of rulings or rules, which are determinate individuals, and multiplicity of place (e.g., different scholars' minds) is not considered. Al-Anbabi provides an analogy: like two weak threads without twisting remain weak, but when twisted they become one strong thread. He then references al-Dawwani and his teachers, who hold that the terms are 'mental utterances' (الألفاظ الذهنية) which are not among the seven categories typically discussed (e.g., vocal utterances). The reasoning is that mental utterances are transient and not intended for themselves, so they cannot be signified. However, this reasoning is critiqued: perhaps 'utterances' includes mental ones, and the deficiency is that the reasoning only addresses external utterances. If mental utterances and the meanings they convey are also considered, the number of probabilities exceeds seven. The author also clarifies that the view that names of sciences are generic names is not based on the philosophical point that things multiply by their place, but because these names are extensible—they increase as knowledge increases, similar to the name 'Usama' for lion which covers both actual and potential individuals. This contrasts with names of books or biographies which are fixed. Finally, a quote from al-Sayyid al-Jurjani introduces a distinction: if the names refer to the rules (قواعد), they are proper names because the rules are identical across minds; if they refer to perceptions (إدراكات), they are generic because perceptions differ by person. The passage ends noting that the case of disposition (ملكة) is similar to perception. The discussion thus refines the classification of terms used in logic and epistemology.

Key Terms
علم الشخص
علم الجنس
القواعد
الإدراكات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, when al-Sayyid al-Jurjani's distinction is applied, what is the status of a science name if it refers to the actual rules (the qawa‘id) in the mind?
A) Proper name, because the rules are considered identical across minds.
B) Generic name, because the rules are universal.
C) Proper name, because perceptions differ.
D) Generic name, because rules multiply with place.

Key Takeaway
A science name can be either a proper or a generic name depending on whether its referent is the universal rules (proper) or the individual perceptions of those rules (generic).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Take the science of 'Tafsir'. According to al-Jurjani's distinction, if one intends by 'Tafsir' the established rules of Qur'anic interpretation (e.g., the principles of interpreting verses), is the name 'Tafsir' a proper name or a generic name? Explain your reasoning using the text.
?
Consider the name 'Maliki fiqh'. If we refer to the specific corpus of Maliki rulings, what is its status? If we refer to the universal concept of Maliki jurisprudence, what changes? Write a brief analysis.

Refuting the Prohibition of Logic: The Reductio Argument
Refutation of an argument against studying logic

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply reductio ad absurdum to refute an argument that uses association with non-Muslims as grounds for prohibition in Islamic legal reasoning.

Core Idea
The refutation of Ibn al-Salah's prohibition is a textbook example of reductio ad absurdum.

In this passage, the commentator refutes the argument that studying logic is forbidden because Jews and Christians engage in it. The refutation is a powerful logical move: if that were a valid reason, then many other essential activities would also be forbidden. This lesson will walk you through the structure of this refutation and its implications for Islamic legal reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the original argument
Ibn al-Salah and al-Nawawi prohibited logic. Some justified it by noting that Jews and Christians occupy themselves with it.
2
Find the refutation
The commentator says this reasoning is refuted: if valid, it would require prohibiting medicine, grammar, eating, drinking, etc., since Jews and Christians also do those.
3
Classify the logical form
This is a reductio ad absurdum: assuming the reasoning leads to an absurd conclusion, so the reasoning must be invalid.
4
Evaluate the refutation
The refutation is sound because it shows the argument proves too much. Note that the commentator does not simply dismiss the prohibition but offers a better justification later.
The better justification is that logic may lead to deviant beliefs, but even that is contested.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَابْنُ الصَّلَاحِ وَالنَّوَاوِي حَرَّمَا، وَقَالَ قَوْمٌ: يَنْبَغِي أَنْ يُعْلَمَ . قَوْلُهُ : (حَرَّمَا ) أَيْ : حَرَّمَا الاِشْتِغَالَ بِهِ، وَوَافَقَهُمَا عَلَى ذَلِكَ كَثِيرٌ مِنَ العُلَمَاءِ . قَالَ بَعْضُهُمْ : وَوَجْهُ تَحْرِيم هَوْلَاءِ الِاشْتَغَالَ بِهِ : أَنَّهُ يَشْتَغِلُ بِهِ اليَهُودُ وَالنَّصَارَى . وَرُدَّ بِأَنَّهُ يَلْزَمُ هَذَا القَائِلُ تَحْرِيمَ الطَّبّ وَالنَّحْوِ ، بَلْ وَالأَكْلِ وَالشُّرْبِ وَغَيْرِهِمَا ؛ لِاشْتِغَالِ اليَهُودِ وَالنَّصَارَى بِذَلِكَ. فَالأَحْسُنُ أَنْ يُقَالَ : وَجْهُ تَحْرِيمِهِمُ الاشْتِغَالَ بِهِ : أَنَّهُ حَيْثُ كَانَ مَخْلُوطاً بِضَلَالَاتِ الفَلَاسِفَةِ يُخْشَى عَلَى الشَّخْصِ إِذَا اشْتَغَلَ بِهِ أَنْ يَتَمَكَّنَ مِنْ قَلْبِهِ بَعْضُ العَقَائِدِ الزَّائِغَةِ، كَمَا وَقَعَ ذَلِكَ لِلمُعْتَزِلَةِ ; كَذَا يُؤخَذُ مِنْ كَلامِ الشَّيْخ المَلْوِيِّ . وَقَدْ يَرِدُ عَلَيْهِ : أَنَّ هَذَا الوَجْهَ لَا يَظْهَرُ فِي مَنْ كَانَ كَامِلَ القَرِيحَةِ مُمَارِسَ السُّنَّةِ وَالْكِتَابِ . وَقَدْ يُجَابُ : بِأَنَّهُمُ الْتَزَمُوا ذَلِكَ حَتَّى بِالنِّسْبَةِ لِمَنْ كَانَ كَذَلِكَ ، وَإِنْ لَمْ يَظْهَرْ فِيهِ مَا ذَكَرَ؛ سَدًّا لِلْبَابِ وَدَرَأ لِلْمَفْسَدَةِ، فَلْيُرَاجَعْ. قَوْلُهُ : (وَقَالَ قَوْمٌ هُمُ الغَزَالِيُّ وَمَنْ تَبِعَهُ . وَقَوْلُهُ : (يَنْبَغِي أَنْ يُعْلَمَ) طَرَقَ فِيهِ الشَّيْخُ المَلوِيُّ احْتِمَالَي الوُجُوبِ وَالنَّدْبِ حَيْثُ قَالَ : يُحْتَمَلُ أَنْ يَكُونَ بِمَعْنَى : يَجِبُ كِفَايَةٌ، وَيُحْتَمَلُ أَنْ يَكُونَ بِمَعْنَى : يُسْتَحَبُّ . لَكِنَّ المُصَنِّفَ جَزَمَ بِحَمْلِهِ عَلَى الِاسْتِحْبَابِ حَيْثُ قَالَ: «وَاسْتَحَبَّهُ الغَزَالِيُّ وَمَنْ تَبِعَهُ».
Ibn al-Salah and al-Nawawi prohibited (studying logic), and a group said: it should be learned. His statement: 'they prohibited' means they prohibited occupying oneself with it, and many scholars agreed with them. Some said: the reason for their prohibition is that Jews and Christians occupy themselves with it. It was refuted that this (argument) would require prohibiting medicine and grammar, and even eating and drinking and other things, because Jews and Christians occupy themselves with that. So it is better to say: the reason for their prohibition of occupying oneself with it is that since it is mixed with the errors of the philosophers, it is feared for a person that if he occupies himself with it, some deviant beliefs may become established in his heart, as happened to the Mu'tazila; this is taken from the words of al-Shaykh al-Malawi. And an objection may be raised against this: that this reason is not apparent in one who is of perfect intellect and well-versed in the Sunnah and the Book. And it may be answered: that they adhered to that even with respect to one who is like that, even if what was mentioned is not apparent in him, as a block to the door and to repel harm; so let it be consulted. His statement: 'and a group said' – they are al-Ghazali and his followers. And his statement: 'it should be learned' – al-Shaykh al-Malawi presented two possibilities of obligation and recommendation, saying: it is possible that it means communal obligation, and it is possible that it means recommended. But the author affirmed that it is interpreted as recommendation, where he said: 'al-Ghazali and his followers considered it recommended.'
Source pages p. 144

Commentary
This passage is a critical expansion on the debate regarding the ruling on studying the science of logic. It begins by stating that Ibn al-Salah and al-Nawawi prohibited it, then mentions that a group (al-Ghazali and his followers) held that it should be learned. The commentator then explains the prohibition: some argued that the reason is that Jews and Christians engage in it. This argument is refuted by noting that the same logic would require prohibiting medicine, grammar, eating, drinking, etc., since those are also done by Jews and Christians. The commentator then offers a better justification: because logic is mixed with philosophical errors, it may lead to deviant beliefs, as happened to the Mu'tazila. An objection follows: this risk does not apply to a person of sound intellect well-versed in the Quran and Sunnah. The response is that they (the prohibitionists) maintain the prohibition even for such a person as a preventive measure. Then the passage turns to the permissive view: al-Ghazali and his followers say 'yanbaghi an yu'lama' (it should be learned). Al-Malawi considers whether this means it is obligatory (communal obligation) or recommended. The author of the main text (likely al-Bajuri) explicitly rules that it is recommended, quoting al-Ghazali's own statement that he considered it recommended. Thus, the passage critically examines the arguments for prohibition and concludes with the preferred opinion of recommendation.

Key Terms
حرم
وجه
رد
لازم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What was the refutation of the argument that studying logic should be prohibited because Jews and Christians engage in it?
A) The argument is weak because logic is beneficial for all.
B) That logic would also require prohibiting medicine, grammar, and even eating and drinking since Jews and Christians also do those.
C) The prohibition only applies to those with weak intellects.
D) Al-Ghazali argued that it is recommended.

Key Takeaway
The argument that logic is prohibited because non-Muslims use it is refuted by showing it leads to absurd consequences, demonstrating the power of reductio ad absurdum in Islamic legal reasoning.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct a reductio ad absurdum against the following argument: 'Studying philosophy is haram because the ancient Greeks were polytheists and they studied it.' Show the absurd consequences.
?
Identify the fallacy in this argument: 'We should not study logic because the Mu'tazila studied it and they fell into error.' Then rephrase the argument as a valid reductio.

Al-Ghazali's Permissive View: The Ambiguity of 'Yanbaghi'
Clarification of the permissive view's legal category

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the legal implications of 'yanbaghi' and identify when it indicates recommendation (istihbab) rather than obligation (wujub).

Core Idea
Al-Ghazali's use of 'yanbaghi' was interpreted as recommendation, not obligation, settling a key ambiguity.

The permissive view on studying logic is attributed to al-Ghazali and his followers. They used the word 'yanbaghi' (it should be learned), which is ambiguous in legal discourse. In this lesson, we examine how commentators clarified that al-Ghazali meant recommendation, not obligation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the permissive statement
Al-Ghazali says: 'yanbaghi an yu'lama' (it should be learned).
2
Understand the ambiguity
In Arabic, 'yanbaghi' can mean both obligation (wujub) and recommendation (istihbab).
It can also be understood as 'it is proper'. Al-Malawi considers both possibilities.
3
See the commentator's analysis
Al-Malawi presents both possibilities: either it means communal obligation (wujub kifaya) or recommendation (istihbab).
4
Note the author's resolution
Al-Bajuri explicitly rules that it means recommendation, quoting al-Ghazali's own statement that he considered it recommended.
This shows that the author adheres to the view of recommendation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَابْنُ الصَّلَاحِ وَالنَّوَاوِي حَرَّمَا، وَقَالَ قَوْمٌ: يَنْبَغِي أَنْ يُعْلَمَ . قَوْلُهُ : (حَرَّمَا ) أَيْ : حَرَّمَا الاِشْتِغَالَ بِهِ، وَوَافَقَهُمَا عَلَى ذَلِكَ كَثِيرٌ مِنَ العُلَمَاءِ . قَالَ بَعْضُهُمْ : وَوَجْهُ تَحْرِيم هَوْلَاءِ الِاشْتَغَالَ بِهِ : أَنَّهُ يَشْتَغِلُ بِهِ اليَهُودُ وَالنَّصَارَى . وَرُدَّ بِأَنَّهُ يَلْزَمُ هَذَا القَائِلُ تَحْرِيمَ الطَّبّ وَالنَّحْوِ ، بَلْ وَالأَكْلِ وَالشُّرْبِ وَغَيْرِهِمَا ؛ لِاشْتِغَالِ اليَهُودِ وَالنَّصَارَى بِذَلِكَ. فَالأَحْسُنُ أَنْ يُقَالَ : وَجْهُ تَحْرِيمِهِمُ الاشْتِغَالَ بِهِ : أَنَّهُ حَيْثُ كَانَ مَخْلُوطاً بِضَلَالَاتِ الفَلَاسِفَةِ يُخْشَى عَلَى الشَّخْصِ إِذَا اشْتَغَلَ بِهِ أَنْ يَتَمَكَّنَ مِنْ قَلْبِهِ بَعْضُ العَقَائِدِ الزَّائِغَةِ، كَمَا وَقَعَ ذَلِكَ لِلمُعْتَزِلَةِ ; كَذَا يُؤخَذُ مِنْ كَلامِ الشَّيْخ المَلْوِيِّ . وَقَدْ يَرِدُ عَلَيْهِ : أَنَّ هَذَا الوَجْهَ لَا يَظْهَرُ فِي مَنْ كَانَ كَامِلَ القَرِيحَةِ مُمَارِسَ السُّنَّةِ وَالْكِتَابِ . وَقَدْ يُجَابُ : بِأَنَّهُمُ الْتَزَمُوا ذَلِكَ حَتَّى بِالنِّسْبَةِ لِمَنْ كَانَ كَذَلِكَ ، وَإِنْ لَمْ يَظْهَرْ فِيهِ مَا ذَكَرَ؛ سَدًّا لِلْبَابِ وَدَرَأ لِلْمَفْسَدَةِ، فَلْيُرَاجَعْ. قَوْلُهُ : (وَقَالَ قَوْمٌ هُمُ الغَزَالِيُّ وَمَنْ تَبِعَهُ . وَقَوْلُهُ : (يَنْبَغِي أَنْ يُعْلَمَ) طَرَقَ فِيهِ الشَّيْخُ المَلوِيُّ احْتِمَالَي الوُجُوبِ وَالنَّدْبِ حَيْثُ قَالَ : يُحْتَمَلُ أَنْ يَكُونَ بِمَعْنَى : يَجِبُ كِفَايَةٌ، وَيُحْتَمَلُ أَنْ يَكُونَ بِمَعْنَى : يُسْتَحَبُّ . لَكِنَّ المُصَنِّفَ جَزَمَ بِحَمْلِهِ عَلَى الِاسْتِحْبَابِ حَيْثُ قَالَ: «وَاسْتَحَبَّهُ الغَزَالِيُّ وَمَنْ تَبِعَهُ».
Ibn al-Salah and al-Nawawi prohibited (studying logic), and a group said: it should be learned. The Five Types of Precedence (أنواع التقدم الخمسة)
Author defines nazari and daruri knowledge, explains five types of precedence with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and distinguish the five types of logical precedence using the criteria and examples provided in this passage.

Core Idea
Precedence is not only temporal; the five types reveal how ideas can depend on each other prior to proof.

The author begins by explaining why tasawwur (conceptualization) is presented before tasdiq (assent) in the matn: because it is prior by nature. This leads to a detailed exposition of the five types of precedence. Understanding these types is essential for grasping the logical order of concepts and the relationships between different categories of knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Identify the five types from the poetic line
Find the line of poetry that lists the five types: precedence of nature, time, cause, rank, and honor. Understand that the first type (nature) is the focus for the tasawwur-tasdiq relationship.
The line: 'تقدّم طبع، والزمان، وعلّة ورتبة أيضاً، والتقدّم للشرف'
2
Understand the first type: natural precedence
The criterion: the prior is needed by the posterior but is not its cause. The example given: the number one in relation to two, and tasawwur in relation to tasdiq. The prior is a prerequisite, not a cause.
This is the type relevant to the ordering of concepts in logic.
3
Learn the third type: causal precedence
The prior is both needed and is a cause of the posterior. Example: movement of the finger causing movement of the ring. However, in external existence they are simultaneous; the precedence is only in mental apprehension.
This shows that causality does not necessarily imply temporal priority.
4
Distinguish the remaining three types
Time precedence: father before son. Rank precedence: imam before follower (also called place precedence). Honor precedence: scholar before ignorant. Note that each has its own criterion: time is chronological, rank is hierarchical, honor is based on virtue.
These are more straightforward and the passage says their criteria are apparent.
5
Apply the criteria to new examples
Given a relationship, determine which type of precedence applies. For instance: the priority of wudhu (ablution) over taharah (purity) – is it by cause, nature, or time? Check the definition: wudhu causes taharah, so it is causal precedence.
This step solidifies the distinction between types.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أنواع العلم الحادث
وَقَدِّمِ الأَوَّلَ عِنْدَ الوَضْعِ لِأَنَّهُ مُقَدَّمٌ بِالطَّبْعِ
قَوْلُهُ : (لِأَنَّهُ مُقَدَّمٌ بِالطَّبْعِ) أَيْ: وَكُلُّ مَا كَانَ مُقَدَّماً بِالطَّبْعِ يُنَاسِبُ أَنْ يُقَدَّمَ فِي الوَضْعِ ؛ لِيُنَاسِبَ الوَضْعُ الطَّبْعَ ، وَهَذَا أَحَدُ أَنْوَاعِ التَّقَدُّمِ الخَمْسَةِ المَنْظُومَةُ فِي قَوْلِ بَعْضِهِمْ : وَخَمْسَةٌ انْوَاعِ التَّقَدُّمِ يَا فَتَى أَقْرَبُهَا بَيْتُ مِنَ الشِّعْرِ وَاعْتَرِفْ تَقَدُّمُ طَبْع "، وَالزَّمَانِ، وَعِلَّةٍ وَرُتْبَةٍ ايضاً، وَالتَّقَدُّمُ لِلشَّرف ) وَقَوْلُهُ: «وَخَمْسَةُ انْوَاعِ التَّقَدُّمِ» يُقْرَأُ بِدَرْجِ الهَمْزَةِ، وَكَذَا قَوْلُهُ: «وَرُتْبَةٍ أَيْضاً كَمَا لَا يَخْفَى عَلَى مَنْ لَهُ إِلْمَامٌ بِفَنِّ العَرُوضِ .
وَضَابِط الأَوَّلِ : أَنْ يَكُونَ المُقَدَّمُ بِحَيْثُ يَحْتَاجُ إِلَيْهِ المُؤَخَّرُ مِنْ غَيْرِ أَنْ يَكُونَ عِلَّةٌ فِيهِ كَالْوَاحِدِ بِالنِّسْبَةِ لِلاثْنَيْنِ ، وَكَذَلِكَ : «التَّصَوُّرُ بِالنِّسْبَةِ لِلتَّصْدِيقِ».
وَضَابِطُ الثَّالِثِ : أَنْ يَكُونَ المُقَدَّمُ بِحَيْثُ يَحْتَاجُ إِلَيْهِ المُؤَخَرُ مَعَ كَوْنِهِ عِلَّةٌ فِيهِ كَ: «حَرَكَةِ الإِصْبَعِ بِالنِّسْبَةِ لِحَرَكَةِ الخَاتَمِ ، فَإِنَّهَا مُقَدَّمَةٌ عَلَيْهَا ، وَهِيَ عِلَّةٌ فِيهَا ، لَكِنْ تَقَدُّمَهَا عَلَيْهَا إِنَّمَا هُوَ فِي التَّعَقُلِ. وَإِلَّا فَهُمَا فِي الوُجُودِ الخَارِجِيِّ مُتَقَارِنَانِ.
وَضَابِط كُلِّ مِنَ الثَّانِي وَالرَّابِعِ وَالخَامِسِ ظَاهِرٌ، وَمِثَالُ الثَّانِي: تَقَدُّمُ الأَبِ عَلَى الابْنِ»، وَمِثَالُ الرَّابِعِ : تَقَدُّمُ الإِمَامِ عَلَى المَأمُومِ، وَعَبَّرَ بَعْضُهُمْ عَنْ هَذَا النَّوْعِ بـ : «التَّقَدُّمِ بِالْمَكَانِ» وَمَثَّلَ لَهُ بِذَلِكَ، وَمِثَالُ الخَامِسِ : تَقَدُّمُ العَالِمِ عَلَى الجَاهِلِ) .
وَاعْلَمْ أَنَّ التَّصْدِيقَ إِنَّمَا يَتَوَفَّفُ عَلَى تَصَوُّرٍ يُنَاسِبُهُ ، فَإِذَا رَأَيْتَ شَبَحاً مِنْ بُعْدِ صَحَ أَنْ تَحْكُمَ عَلَيْهِ بِأَنَّهُ شَاغِلٌ فَرَاعاً ؛ لِأَنَّهُ قَدْ وُجِدَ التَّصَوُّرُ الَّذِي يُنَاسِبُهُ، وَهُوَ تَصَوُّرُهُ بِكَوْنِهِ جِسْماً، وَلَا يَتَوَقَّفُ عَلَى أَنْ تَتَصَوَّرَ أَنَّهُ إِنْسَانُ، أَوْ فَرَسٌ مَثَلاً .
نَعَمْ؛ لَوْ أَرَدْتَ أَنْ تَحْكُمَ عَلَيْهِ بِأَنَّهُ مُتَحَرِّكُ مَثَلاً ، لَمْ يَسُغْ لَكَ ذَلِكَ حَتَّى تَتَصَرُّرَ مَا ذُكِرَ ؛ أَفَادَهُ الشَّيْخُ المَلوِيُّ فِي شَرْحِهِ الكَبِيرِ مَعَ زِيَادَةٍ ) .
قَوْلُهُ : (وَ النَّظَرِي» ... إلخ) بِإِسْكَانِ «اليَاءِ» لِلْوَزْنِ .
وَغَرَضُ المُصَنِّفِ بِذَلِكَ : تَعْرِيفُ كُلِّ مِنَ النَّظَرِيِّ وَالضَّرُورِي اللَّذَيْنِ هُمَا قِسْمَانِ لِلعِلْمِ الشَّامِل لِكُلِّ مِنَ التَّصَوُّرِ وَالتَّصْدِيقِ، فَيُتَحَصَّلُ مِنْ ذَلِكَ أَرْبَعَةُ أَقْسَامٍ كَمَا مَرَّ .
هَذَا هُوَ الرَّاجِحُ ، وَوَرَاءَهُ ثَلَاثَةُ أَقْوَالٍ :
(۱) - أَحَدُهَا : مَا قَالَهُ الفَخْرُ مِنْ أَنَّ التَّصَوُّرَ ضَرُورِيٌّ لَيْسَ إِلَّا ، وَأَنَّ التَّصْدِيقَ يَنْقَسِمُ : إِلَى نَظَرِي، وَإِلَى ضَرُورِيٌّ.
(٢) - ثَانِيهَا : أَنَّ العِلْمَ كُلُّهُ ضَرُورِيٌّ .
(۳) - ثَالِتُهَا : أَنَّهُ نَظَرِيٌّ . أَفَادَهُ الشَّيْخُ المَلَّوِيُّ ، وَقَدْ ذَكَرَ تَوْجِيهَ هَذِهِ الأَقْوَالِ مَعَ بَيَانِ أَنَّ الخِلَافَ لَفْظِيٌّ فِي شَرْحِهِ الكَبِيرِ»
Types of originated knowledge. And present the first in place because it is prior by nature. His saying: (because it is prior by nature) meaning: and everything that is prior by nature is appropriate to be presented in place, so that the order corresponds to nature. This is one of the five types of precedence versified in the saying of some: 'Five types of precedence, O youth, the closest of them is a line of poetry, and acknowledge: precedence of nature, time, cause, rank also, and precedence for honor.' And his saying: 'Five types of precedence' is read with elision of the hamza, and similarly his saying: 'and rank also' as is not hidden from one who has familiarity with the art of prosody. The criterion of the first: that the prior be such that the posterior needs it without it being a cause of it, like the one in relation to two, and likewise: conceptualization in relation to assent. The criterion of the third: that the prior be such that the posterior needs it while it is a cause of it, like the movement of the finger in relation to the movement of the ring, for it is prior to it and is a cause of it, but its priority over it is only in mental apprehension; otherwise they are simultaneous in external existence. The criterion of each of the second, fourth, and fifth is apparent. An example of the second: the priority of the father over the son. An example of the fourth: the priority of the imam over the follower, and some have expressed this type as 'priority by place' and gave that as an example. An example of the fifth: the priority of the scholar over the ignorant. And know that assent depends only on a conceptualization that is suitable to it. So if you see a figure from afar, it is valid for you to judge it as occupying space, because there exists the conceptualization that suits it, namely conceptualizing it as a body, and it does not depend on your conceptualizing it as a human or a horse, for example. Yes, if you wanted to judge it as moving, for example, that would not be permissible for you until you conceptualize what was mentioned. This was imparted by Shaykh al-Malawi in his Great Commentary with addition. His saying: (And the theoretical... etc.) with the ya' made silent for meter. And the author's purpose with this is to define each of the theoretical and the necessary, which are two divisions of knowledge that encompasses both conceptualization and assent, so four divisions are obtained as previously mentioned. This is the preponderant view, and beyond it are three opinions: (1) One of them: what al-Fakhr said, that conceptualization is only necessary, and that assent is divided into theoretical and necessary. (2) Second: that all knowledge is necessary. (3) Third: that it is theoretical. Shaykh al-Malawi imparted this, and he mentioned the explanation of these opinions with the clarification that the dispute is verbal in his Great Commentary, so let one consult it.
Source pages p. 157

Commentary
This passage is a definitional and epistemological expansion from the hashiya of al-Bajuri and al-Bannani on the Sullam. It begins by explaining why the author of the matn prioritized tasawwur (conceptualization) over tasdiq (assent) in presentation: because it is prior by nature (taqaddum bil-tab'). The commentator then clarifies that natural precedence is one of five types of precedence, listing them with a poetic verse: precedence by nature, time, cause, rank, and honor. He provides criteria and examples for each. The first type (natural precedence) is when the prior is needed by the posterior without being its cause, exemplified by the number one relative to the number two, and by tasawwur relative to tasdiq. The third type (causal precedence) is when the prior is needed and is also a cause, like the finger's movement causing the ring's movement, though they are simultaneous in external existence. The second (time precedence) is exemplified by father before son, the fourth (rank precedence) by imam before follower (also called place precedence), and the fifth (honor precedence) by scholar before ignoramus. The passage then shifts to clarify that tasdiq depends on an appropriate tasawwur, but not necessarily a detailed one; e.g., seeing a distant shape, one can judge it as occupying space based on the simple conceptualization of it as a body, without needing to conceive it as human or horse. However, judging it as moving requires more specific conceptualization. This is from al-Malawi's commentary. Finally, the author defines theoretical knowledge (nazari) as that which requires reflection, and necessary (daruri) as its opposite. The purpose is to derive four divisions of knowledge: necessary conceptualization, theoretical conceptualization, necessary assent, and theoretical assent. This is the preponderant view. Three weaker opinions are noted: (1) al-Fakhr al-Razi held that conceptualization is only necessary and assent divides; (2) all knowledge is necessary; (3) all knowledge is theoretical. The commentary notes that al-Malawi explained these as merely verbal disputes.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which type of precedence is exemplified by the relationship between tasawwur and tasdiq?
A) Precedence by time
B) Precedence by nature
C) Precedence by cause
D) Precedence by honor

Key Takeaway
The five types of precedence—nature, time, cause, rank, and honor—provide a framework for understanding logical dependence that goes beyond mere time.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Take the relationship between 'wudhu' and 'taharah' (ritual purity). Is wudhu prior to taharah by nature, time, cause, rank, or honor? Explain using the criteria.
?
In a debate, a scholar says 'The evidence is prior to the conclusion by nature.' How would you check if this is correct? Use the definition from the passage.

Hadd and Rasm
intermediate
8 min
Theoretical vs Necessary Knowledge (النظري والضروري)
Author defines nazari and daruri knowledge, explains five types of precedence with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify any piece of knowledge as theoretical (nazari) or necessary (daruri) and explain the fourfold division of knowledge.

Core Idea
All knowledge is either theoretical (requiring reflection) or necessary (self-evident), and this applies to both conceptualization and assent.

After discussing precedence, the author defines the two fundamental types of knowledge: theoretical (nazari) and necessary (daruri). This division applies to both conceptualization and assent, yielding four categories. Understanding this classification is crucial for evaluating the epistemic status of claims in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Define nazari and daruri
Nazari (theoretical) knowledge requires reflection and deliberation (ta'ammul) to be obtained. Daruri (necessary) knowledge is obtained without reflection; it is self-evident or immediate.
These definitions are complementary and exhaustive of the category of 'ilm (knowledge).
2
Recall the four divisions from previous lesson
The author says knowledge encompasses both tasawwur and tasdiq. Combining with the nazari/daruri dichotomy gives: daruri tasawwur, nazari tasawwur, daruri tasdiq, nazari tasdiq. This is the preponderant view.
This is the standard classification used in later Islamic logic.
3
Understand the condition for tasdiq
Tasdiq (assent) depends on an appropriate tasawwur (conceptualization), but not necessarily a detailed one. Example: seeing a distant shape, you can judge it as occupying space based on the simple tasawwur of it as a body.
This shows that a minimal conceptualization suffices for some assents.
4
Note the three weaker opinions
Three alternative views: (1) al-Fakhr al-Razi said tasawwur is only daruri and tasdiq divides into nazari/daruri. (2) All knowledge is daruri. (3) All knowledge is nazari. The commentary mentions these but says the dispute is verbal.
Verbal dispute means the disagreement is about terminology, not substance.
5
Identify examples from Islamic sciences
For instance, the knowledge that Allah is one (concept) is daruri for most Muslims, while the proof of His attributes may be nazari. In fiqh, the obligation of prayer is daruri, but the specific rulings of salah might be nazari for a new issue.
Apply the definition to real Islamic knowledge.

أنواع العلم الحادث
وَقَدِّمِ الأَوَّلَ عِنْدَ الوَضْعِ لِأَنَّهُ مُقَدَّمٌ بِالطَّبْعِ
قَوْلُهُ : (لِأَنَّهُ مُقَدَّمٌ بِالطَّبْعِ) أَيْ: وَكُلُّ مَا كَانَ مُقَدَّماً بِالطَّبْعِ يُنَاسِبُ أَنْ يُقَدَّمَ فِي الوَضْعِ ؛ لِيُنَاسِبَ الوَضْعُ الطَّبْعَ ، وَهَذَا أَحَدُ أَنْوَاعِ التَّقَدُّمِ الخَمْسَةِ المَنْظُومَةُ فِي قَوْلِ بَعْضِهِمْ : وَخَمْسَةٌ انْوَاعِ التَّقَدُّمِ يَا فَتَى أَقْرَبُهَا بَيْتُ مِنَ الشِّعْرِ وَاعْتَرِفْ تَقَدُّمُ طَبْع "، وَالزَّمَانِ، وَعِلَّةٍ وَرُتْبَةٍ ايضاً، وَالتَّقَدُّمُ لِلشَّرف ) وَقَوْلُهُ: «وَخَمْسَةُ انْوَاعِ التَّقَدُّمِ» يُقْرَأُ بِدَرْجِ الهَمْزَةِ، وَكَذَا قَوْلُهُ: «وَرُتْبَةٍ أَيْضاً كَمَا لَا يَخْفَى عَلَى مَنْ لَهُ إِلْمَامٌ بِفَنِّ العَرُوضِ .
وَضَابِط الأَوَّلِ : أَنْ يَكُونَ المُقَدَّمُ بِحَيْثُ يَحْتَاجُ إِلَيْهِ المُؤَخَّرُ مِنْ غَيْرِ أَنْ يَكُونَ عِلَّةٌ فِيهِ كَالْوَاحِدِ بِالنِّسْبَةِ لِلاثْنَيْنِ ، وَكَذَلِكَ : «التَّصَوُّرُ بِالنِّسْبَةِ لِلتَّصْدِيقِ».
وَضَابِطُ الثَّالِثِ : أَنْ يَكُونَ المُقَدَّمُ بِحَيْثُ يَحْتَاجُ إِلَيْهِ المُؤَخَرُ مَعَ كَوْنِهِ عِلَّةٌ فِيهِ كَ: «حَرَكَةِ الإِصْبَعِ بِالنِّسْبَةِ لِحَرَكَةِ الخَاتَمِ ، فَإِنَّهَا مُقَدَّمَةٌ عَلَيْهَا ، وَهِيَ عِلَّةٌ فِيهَا ، لَكِنْ تَقَدُّمَهَا عَلَيْهَا إِنَّمَا هُوَ فِي التَّعَقُلِ. وَإِلَّا فَهُمَا فِي الوُجُودِ الخَارِجِيِّ مُتَقَارِنَانِ.
وَضَابِط كُلِّ مِنَ الثَّانِي وَالرَّابِعِ وَالخَامِسِ ظَاهِرٌ، وَمِثَالُ الثَّانِي: تَقَدُّمُ الأَبِ عَلَى الابْنِ»، وَمِثَالُ الرَّابِعِ : تَقَدُّمُ الإِمَامِ عَلَى المَأمُومِ، وَعَبَّرَ بَعْضُهُمْ عَنْ هَذَا النَّوْعِ بـ : «التَّقَدُّمِ بِالْمَكَانِ» وَمَثَّلَ لَهُ بِذَلِكَ، وَمِثَالُ الخَامِسِ : تَقَدُّمُ العَالِمِ عَلَى الجَاهِلِ) .
وَاعْلَمْ أَنَّ التَّصْدِيقَ إِنَّمَا يَتَوَفَّفُ عَلَى تَصَوُّرٍ يُنَاسِبُهُ ، فَإِذَا رَأَيْتَ شَبَحاً مِنْ بُعْدِ صَحَ أَنْ تَحْكُمَ عَلَيْهِ بِأَنَّهُ شَاغِلٌ فَرَاعاً ؛ لِأَنَّهُ قَدْ وُجِدَ التَّصَوُّرُ الَّذِي يُنَاسِبُهُ، وَهُوَ تَصَوُّرُهُ بِكَوْنِهِ جِسْماً، وَلَا يَتَوَقَّفُ عَلَى أَنْ تَتَصَوَّرَ أَنَّهُ إِنْسَانُ، أَوْ فَرَسٌ مَثَلاً .
نَعَمْ؛ لَوْ أَرَدْتَ أَنْ تَحْكُمَ عَلَيْهِ بِأَنَّهُ مُتَحَرِّكُ مَثَلاً ، لَمْ يَسُغْ لَكَ ذَلِكَ حَتَّى تَتَصَرُّرَ مَا ذُكِرَ ؛ أَفَادَهُ الشَّيْخُ المَلوِيُّ فِي شَرْحِهِ الكَبِيرِ مَعَ زِيَادَةٍ ) .
قَوْلُهُ : (وَ النَّظَرِي» ... إلخ) بِإِسْكَانِ «اليَاءِ» لِلْوَزْنِ .
وَغَرَضُ المُصَنِّفِ بِذَلِكَ : تَعْرِيفُ كُلِّ مِنَ النَّظَرِيِّ وَالضَّرُورِي اللَّذَيْنِ هُمَا قِسْمَانِ لِلعِلْمِ الشَّامِل لِكُلِّ مِنَ التَّصَوُّرِ وَالتَّصْدِيقِ، فَيُتَحَصَّلُ مِنْ ذَلِكَ أَرْبَعَةُ أَقْسَامٍ كَمَا مَرَّ .
هَذَا هُوَ الرَّاجِحُ ، وَوَرَاءَهُ ثَلَاثَةُ أَقْوَالٍ :
(۱) - أَحَدُهَا : مَا قَالَهُ الفَخْرُ مِنْ أَنَّ التَّصَوُّرَ ضَرُورِيٌّ لَيْسَ إِلَّا ، وَأَنَّ التَّصْدِيقَ يَنْقَسِمُ : إِلَى نَظَرِي، وَإِلَى ضَرُورِيٌّ.
(٢) - ثَانِيهَا : أَنَّ العِلْمَ كُلُّهُ ضَرُورِيٌّ .
(۳) - ثَالِتُهَا : أَنَّهُ نَظَرِيٌّ . أَفَادَهُ الشَّيْخُ المَلَّوِيُّ ، وَقَدْ ذَكَرَ تَوْجِيهَ هَذِهِ الأَقْوَالِ مَعَ بَيَانِ أَنَّ الخِلَافَ لَفْظِيٌّ فِي شَرْحِهِ الكَبِيرِ»
Types of originated knowledge. And present the first in place because it is prior by nature. His saying: (because it is prior by nature) meaning: and everything that is prior by nature is appropriate to be presented in place, so that the order corresponds to nature. This is one of the five types of precedence versified in the saying of some: 'Five types of precedence, O youth, the closest of them is a line of poetry, and acknowledge: precedence of nature, time, cause, rank also, and precedence for honor.' And his saying: 'Five types of precedence' is read with elision of the hamza, and similarly his saying: 'and rank also' as is not hidden from one who has familiarity with the art of prosody. The criterion of the first: that the prior be such that the posterior needs it without it being a cause of it, like the one in relation to two, and likewise: conceptualization in relation to assent. The criterion of the third: that the prior be such that the posterior needs it while it is a cause of it, like the movement of the finger in relation to the movement of the ring, for it is prior to it and is a cause of it, but its priority over it is only in mental apprehension; otherwise they are simultaneous in external existence. The criterion of each of the second, fourth, and fifth is apparent. An example of the second: the priority of the father over the son. An example of the fourth: the priority of the imam over the follower, and some have expressed this type as 'priority by place' and gave that as an example. An example of the fifth: the priority of the scholar over the ignorant. And know that assent depends only on a conceptualization that is suitable to it. So if you see a figure from afar, it is valid for you to judge it as occupying space, because there exists the conceptualization that suits it, namely conceptualizing it as a body, and it does not depend on your conceptualizing it as a human or a horse, for example. Yes, if you wanted to judge it as moving, for example, that would not be permissible for you until you conceptualize what was mentioned. This was imparted by Shaykh al-Malawi in his Great Commentary with addition. His saying: (And the theoretical... etc.) with the ya' made silent for meter. And the author's purpose with this is to define each of the theoretical and the necessary, which are two divisions of knowledge that encompasses both conceptualization and assent, so four divisions are obtained as previously mentioned. This is the preponderant view, and beyond it are three opinions: (1) One of them: what al-Fakhr said, that conceptualization is only necessary, and that assent is divided into theoretical and necessary. (2) Second: that all knowledge is necessary. (3) Third: that it is theoretical. Shaykh al-Malawi imparted this, and he mentioned the explanation of these opinions with the clarification that the dispute is verbal in his Great Commentary, so let one consult it.
Source pages p. 157

Commentary
This passage is a definitional and epistemological expansion from the hashiya of al-Bajuri and al-Bannani on the Sullam. It begins by explaining why the author of the matn prioritized tasawwur (conceptualization) over tasdiq (assent) in presentation: because it is prior by nature (taqaddum bil-tab'). The commentator then clarifies that natural precedence is one of five types of precedence, listing them with a poetic verse: precedence by nature, time, cause, rank, and honor. He provides criteria and examples for each. The first type (natural precedence) is when the prior is needed by the posterior without being its cause, exemplified by the number one relative to the number two, and by tasawwur relative to tasdiq. The third type (causal precedence) is when the prior is needed and is also a cause, like the finger's movement causing the ring's movement, though they are simultaneous in external existence. The second (time precedence) is exemplified by father before son, the fourth (rank precedence) by imam before follower (also called place precedence), and the fifth (honor precedence) by scholar before ignoramus. The passage then shifts to clarify that tasdiq depends on an appropriate tasawwur, but not necessarily a detailed one; e.g., seeing a distant shape, one can judge it as occupying space based on the simple conceptualization of it as a body, without needing to conceive it as human or horse. However, judging it as moving requires more specific conceptualization. This is from al-Malawi's commentary. Finally, the author defines theoretical knowledge (nazari) as that which requires reflection, and necessary (daruri) as its opposite. The purpose is to derive four divisions of knowledge: necessary conceptualization, theoretical conceptualization, necessary assent, and theoretical assent. This is the preponderant view. Three weaker opinions are noted: (1) al-Fakhr al-Razi held that conceptualization is only necessary and assent divides; (2) all knowledge is necessary; (3) all knowledge is theoretical. The commentary notes that al-Malawi explained these as merely verbal disputes.

النَّظَرِي
الضَّرُورِي
التَّصَوُّر
التَّصْدِيق

According to the passage, which of the following statements about knowledge divisions is the preponderant view?
A) All knowledge is necessary
B) All knowledge is theoretical
C) Conceptualization is only necessary, assent divides

Key Takeaway
The distinction between theoretical and necessary knowledge is fundamental, and it applies to both conceptualization and assent, forming a fourfold division that underpins Islamic epistemology.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Classify the following statements as nazari or daruri, and state whether they are tasawwur or tasdiq: (a) 'God is one' (the concept of oneness), (b) 'The Quran is the word of God' (assent), (c) 'Prayer is obligatory' (assent). Provide reasoning from the passage.
?

Hadd and Rasm
intermediate
5 min
Theoretical and Necessary Knowledge: The Broad Scope of Reflection
Defines theoretical and necessary knowledge and broadens the scope of reflection

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish theoretical from necessary knowledge and identify the methods that produce theoretical knowledge.

Core Idea
Theoretical knowledge requires reflection; necessary knowledge is self-evident — and reflection includes induction and analogy.

Al-Bajuri begins by defining theoretical knowledge as that which requires reflection (tamul), while necessary knowledge is its opposite. However, he warns that 'reflection' must be understood broadly. If it were limited to formal syllogism, the definition would exclude other valid methods like induction and analogy, which are central to Islamic jurisprudence.

Guided Path
1
Locate the definition of nazari
In the Arabic, find 'al-nazari: ma ihtaja li al-tamul'. Note that nazari is defined by its need for reflection.
2
Identify the opposite definition of daruri
Daruri is the opposite of nazari; it does not require reflection and is self-evident.
3
Understand the commentator's warning
Al-Bajuri states that tamul should not be limited to the technical sense of syllogism. Otherwise, the definition would not include knowledge from induction and analogy.
4
Examine the examples
Induction: 'Every animal moves its lower jaw when chewing' (from observing individuals). Analogy: Imam al-Shafi'i's ruling that nabidh is forbidden like wine.
5
Explain why this matters
These examples show that theoretical knowledge in Islamic sciences includes multiple reasoning methods, not just syllogism.

Source Text

وَاعْلَمْ أَنَّ التَّصْدِيقَ إِنَّمَا يَتَوَفَّفُ عَلَى تَصَوُّرٍ يُنَاسِبُهُ ، فَإِذَا رَأَيْتَ شَبَحاً مِنْ بُعْدِ صَحَ أَنْ تَحْكُمَ عَلَيْهِ بِأَنَّهُ شَاغِلٌ فَرَاعاً ؛ لِأَنَّهُ قَدْ وُجِدَ التَّصَوُّرُ الَّذِي يُنَاسِبُهُ، وَهُوَ تَصَوُّرُهُ بِكَوْنِهِ جِسْماً، وَلَا يَتَوَقَّفُ عَلَى أَنْ تَتَصَوَّرَ أَنَّهُ إِنْسَانُ، أَوْ فَرَسٌ مَثَلاً .
نَعَمْ؛ لَوْ أَرَدْتَ أَنْ تَحْكُمَ عَلَيْهِ بِأَنَّهُ مُتَحَرِّكُ مَثَلاً ، لَمْ يَسُغْ لَكَ ذَلِكَ حَتَّى تَتَصَوَّرَ مَا ذُكِرَ ؛ أَفَادَهُ الشَّيْخُ المَلَّوِيُّ فِي شَرْحِهِ الكَبِيرِ مَعَ زِيَادَةٍ ) .
قَوْلُهُ : (وَ النَّظَرِي» ... إلخ) بِإِسْكَانِ «اليَاءِ» لِلْوَزْنِ .
